SLAMMING DOOR                                    HAZARD 1
MECHANICAL  TRAP
Stealth DC 17 (trained)
Description Pressure-sensitive panels in the floor connect to a stone slab hidden in a hallway‚Äôs ceiling.
Disable DC 15 Thievery (trained) on the floor panels before the slab falls <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.136,t=0.071,r=0.517,b=0.187), with ID d5a9a72b-8e94-4442-b070-fe0eed5de606 -->

AC 16; Fort +10, Ref +2  
Hardness 5; HP 20 (BT 10); Immunities critical hits, object immunities, precision damage <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.187,r=0.514,b=0.233), with ID ab687758-6703-43f8-84ca-fbcd63cdd19d -->

**Slam Shut** ‚ôªÔ∏è **Trigger** Pressure is placed on any floor tile; **Effect** The door falls, closing off the hallway. The stone slab deals 3d8 bludgeoning damage to anyone beneath or adjacent to the slab when it drops and pushes them out of its space in a random direction. A creature that succeeds at a DC 17 Reflex save takes no damage and rolls out of the way in a random direction. On a critical success, they can choose the direction.

Lifting the fallen slab requires a successful DC 25 Athletics check. Hitting the floor panels triggers the trap. The slab uses the same AC and saves as the trap, but it has Hardness 12, HP 48 (BT 24). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.235,r=0.516,b=0.403), with ID 39905657-0a97-40f5-ba6b-d507087ac194 -->

## Complex Hazards
Complex hazards function similarly to monsters during encounters, as they roll initiative and have actions of their own, though these are usually automated in a routine. Complex hazards can be used in encounters to take the place of a creature of the same level, and are worth commensurate XP (see page 57). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.241,r=0.866,b=0.360), with ID c8dc3c3e-35a7-4d7e-88f6-7b993cdcb0ba -->

DANCE OF DEATH                                    HAZARD 16

COMPLEX   HAUNT

Stealth +32 (master)
Description An eerie orchestra compels all who hear it to dance until they collapse from exhaustion. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.074,r=0.514,b=0.157), with ID c1a788b4-8a48-4e20-ab59-b8a3c9d09571 -->

Disable DC 42 Intimidation (expert) three times to frighten dancers and spectral musicians alike away from participating in the deadly performance, DC 40 Performance (master) twice to produce a tune discordant enough to disrupt the compulsion, or DC 42 Religion (master) three times to banish the spirits with prayers <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.157,r=0.515,b=0.248), with ID cb345deb-d56c-453a-9c71-8a5f7f899136 -->

Prelude üîÑ (auditory, incapacitation, mental, occult) Trigger A creature approaches within 30 feet of the orchestra; Effect The orchestra compels all creatures that can hear it to begin dancing. Each creature must attempt a DC 41 Will save, with the following effects. The haunt then rolls initiative. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.249,r=0.514,b=0.326), with ID 8c0f6446-9f1b-45eb-8f22-aebab8a6f4fd -->

Critical Success The creature is unaffected.
Success The creature is off-guard and cannot use reactions. Additionally, it must spend 1 of its actions each round dancing. Dancing is a move action that allows the creature to Stride up to half its Speed. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.149,t=0.327,r=0.513,b=0.403), with ID d3f5cb33-64be-4e76-977c-88846e3ebb72 -->

**Failure** As success, except the creature must spend 2 of its actions each round dancing. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.153,t=0.403,r=0.513,b=0.433), with ID 295cc082-6f94-4bb5-b47e-fc2f32bada2e -->

**Critical Failure** As failure, except the creature must spend 3 of its actions each round dancing. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.147,t=0.433,r=0.513,b=0.463), with ID fb85bf8a-7e3d-444e-a54b-49f07a948028 -->

Routine (1 action; auditory, incapacitation, mental, occult) The orchestra performs a raucous tune, compelling all creatures that can hear it to spend actions dancing. Each round, creature must attempt a DC 37 Will save; the results of this save modify the number of actions that the creature must spend dancing each round. If this would cause the creature to spend more actions dancing than it can use on its turn, the creature takes 10d6 damage (or double that on a critical failure) from moving faster than its body can manage. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.141,t=0.464,r=0.514,b=0.602), with ID 218e6152-3c18-4ab5-a7e7-7d2ce75b636f -->

**Critical Success** The creature decreases the number of actions it must spend dancing by 1. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.152,t=0.602,r=0.514,b=0.633), with ID 4cfd9986-c389-4e79-ba1c-5022dd45ea69 -->

**Success** No effect.  
**Failure** The creature increases the actions it must spend dancing by 1. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.155,t=0.633,r=0.512,b=0.677), with ID cd321624-517c-4ace-8834-e9cd50fbf35c -->

**Critical Failure** The creature increases the actions it must spend dancing by 2. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.151,t=0.679,r=0.513,b=0.708), with ID 83f23c3f-6cfb-49f5-9299-ab78f7972d9b -->

**Reset** The eerie orchestra spends an hour retuning its phantasmal instruments, after which it is ready to begin its routine again. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.141,t=0.708,r=0.512,b=0.740), with ID f4675a19-f572-4afa-b7d4-0ec4fa5105b7 -->

DROWNING PIT                                      HAZARD 3
COMPLEX  MECHANICAL  TRAP
Stealth +10 (trained); DC 22 (expert) to notice the water spouts once the pit opens
Description A trapdoor covers a 10-foot-square pit that‚Äôs 30 feet deep and has 5 feet of water at the bottom. Four water spouts in the walls connect to hidden water tanks. Each water spout extends out of a different wall, 6 inches from the top of the pit. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.750,r=0.513,b=0.891), with ID cc7d3cc2-f8f8-4c6f-b306-046196a5aa34 -->

**Disable** DC 18 Thievery (trained) to seal each water spout, DC 22 Thievery (trained) to open the trapdoor, or DC 22 Athletics to Force Open the trapdoor <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.141,t=0.892,r=0.513,b=0.940), with ID 6dd7d5a7-17a0-4ecd-b911-023cac75c380 -->

AC 19; Fort +8, Ref +5  
Trapdoor Hardness 15; Trapdoor HP 60 (BT 30); Spout Hardness 8; Spout HP 32 (BT 16); Immunities critical hits, object immunities, precision damage <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.079,r=0.905,b=0.141), with ID 58c2ceb6-4319-4719-b016-3f89ec149cd1 -->

Pitfall üîÑ Trigger A creature walks onto the trapdoor; Effect The triggering creature falls in and takes damage from the fall, reduced by 5 feet for falling into the water (typically 12 bludgeoning damage). A creature can Grab an Edge to avoid falling (Player Core 419). The trapdoor then slams shut, and the hazard rolls initiative. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.142,r=0.903,b=0.233), with ID ea8d9166-4b68-4126-bc9f-bcc1c97ddf2f -->

Routine (4 actions) The trap loses 1 action each turn for each disabled water spout. On each of the trap‚Äôs actions, a spout pours water, increasing the depth of the water by 5 feet. Once the pit is full of water, the pit stops using actions, but creatures in the pit begin drowning (Player Core 437). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.234,r=0.903,b=0.325), with ID 1d430402-55e8-4742-ac64-2d23afce8829 -->

**Reset** The trap can be reset if the door is manually reengaged and the water tanks are refilled; it can be reset without draining the pit, but doing so renders the pit less effective. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.532,t=0.326,r=0.903,b=0.387), with ID 6b8ad954-51e9-49bb-8616-063ecb34ce62 -->

ETERNAL FLAME                                    HAZARD 7
COMPLEX  HAUNT
Stealth +18 (expert)
Description A raging spectral inferno arises out of thin air, strengthening all undead creatures within its area. This haunt most often arises from the charred remains of a group of three people who burned to death, whether in a terrible accident or a deliberate execution, and their unavenged souls burn with rage. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.398,r=0.904,b=0.539), with ID f68ee6c5-07d2-4b2d-b9a3-9bf6a31817ba -->

**Disable** DC 27 Diplomacy (expert) to temporarily calm the rage of one of the three spirits, or DC 30 Religion (trained) to exorcise one of the spirits; three total successes are required to disable the haunt <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.541,r=0.903,b=0.601), with ID 9cac77a6-2022-4f31-8435-1f3ec3bd461d -->

Searing Agony üîÅ (mental) Trigger A living creature approaches within 10 feet of the remains of a victim of the original fire; Effect Memories of the pain suffered by the fire‚Äôs past victims assault the triggering creature‚Äôs mind. The creature must attempt a DC 25 Will save, and the haunt then rolls initiative. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.532,t=0.602,r=0.904,b=0.692), with ID 2ea2bc72-39ba-42bd-9193-69d362733c1c -->

Critical Success The creature is unaffected.
Success The creature is sickened 1.
Failure The creature is sickened 2.
Critical Failure The creature is sickened 3, and it is off-
  guard for as long as it remains within the haunt‚Äôs area
  and for 3 rounds thereafter. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.541,t=0.694,r=0.902,b=0.784), with ID 887d0c93-9d43-42b6-aba2-de4ad84952b2 -->

Routine (1 action) Phantom flames rage across the haunted area, dealing 4d6 fire damage to each living creature within the area (DC 23 basic Will save). Undead creatures in the area are infused with flames for the following round. They gain the fire trait and immunity to fire, and all their attacks deal an additional 1d6 fire damage. Objects in the area are unaffected. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.533,t=0.785,r=0.902,b=0.891), with ID b6eb7f40-485a-417b-8210-71a2a782233c -->

**Reset** The flames cease 1 minute after all living creatures leave the area, but after 1 hour, the anger and pain simmer up and the haunt is ready to trigger again. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.533,t=0.893,r=0.902,b=0.939), with ID 47417f61-0b36-40df-8779-9e1c99c1b5d6 -->

QUICKSAND                                    HAZARD 3
COMPLEX  ENVIRONMENTAL
Stealth +12 (trained) (or ‚Äì10 and no minimum proficiency if the
  surface is disturbed)
Description A 15-foot-wide patch of water and sand attempts
  to submerge creatures that step onto it.
Disable DC 18 Survival (trained) to disturb the surface
Submerge  Trigger A Huge or smaller creature walks onto
  the quicksand; Effect The triggering creature sinks into the
  quicksand up to its waist. The quicksand rolls initiative if it
  hasn‚Äôt already. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.182,r=0.861,b=0.355), with ID 1d9dee45-d0d7-4f0a-a4a1-fd85589bc07b -->

Routine (1 action) On its initiative, the quicksand pulls down each creature within it. A creature that was submerged up to its waist becomes submerged up to its neck, and a creature that was submerged up to its neck is pulled under and has to hold its breath to avoid suffocation (Player Core 437). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.356,r=0.861,b=0.448), with ID 7297ea6f-3e7d-4b3d-8d70-ad5faf2dc6c0 -->

A creature in the quicksand can attempt a DC 20 Athletics check to Swim to either raise itself by one step if it‚Äôs submerged to its neck or worse, or to move 5 feet if it‚Äôs submerged only up to its waist. On a critical failure, the creature is pulled down one step. A creature that Swims out of the quicksand escapes the hazard and is prone in a space adjacent to the quicksand patch. Other creatures can Aid the creature, typically by using a rope or similar aid, or attempt to pull the creature out with their own DC 20 Athletics check, with the same results as if the creature attempted the check. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.495,t=0.448,r=0.862,b=0.616), with ID 4639dffa-3e0f-4657-a701-e65898d1af27 -->

**Reset** The hazard still submerges anyone who walks in, but the surface doesn‚Äôt become hidden again until it settles over the course of 24 hours. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.617,r=0.860,b=0.664), with ID 8d2576b8-707c-4164-ba43-ce3de1998b2a -->

UPGRADED SUMMONING RUNES  
You can make a summoning rune of nearly any level. It summons a creature of a level equal to the trap's level. Use the DCs by Level table on page 53 to determine the Thievery DC and spell DC, using the trap's level and applying a hard adjustment (+2). The Stealth modifier for the trap is equal to this number ‚Äì 10. Stronger summoning runes usually require expert proficiency or better in Perception to find, and they might require a higher proficiency rank in Thievery to disable. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.072,r=0.514,b=0.265), with ID 667674da-0def-44b7-bbc8-17f4ab8f9182 -->

Rising Pillar Reaction Trigger A creature steps on one of the trapped floor tiles; Effect The trap pops up in a grid intersection and makes a spinning blade Strike against one adjacent creature (if any), then rolls initiative. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.277,r=0.515,b=0.341), with ID 98abd531-c0e6-4908-8918-fda0e215d8e4 -->

Routine (3 actions) The trap uses its first action to make a spinning blade Strike against each adjacent creature, its second action to move straight in a random direction (roll 1d4 to determine the direction), and its third to make a spinning blade Strike against each adjacent creature. This trap doesn‚Äôt take a multiple attack penalty.
Speed 10 feet <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.342,r=0.516,b=0.445), with ID eecd28f2-da7e-4222-94e5-ce1a73e4a94e -->

Melee ‚óÜ spinning blade +12, Damage 2d10+5 slashing  
Reset The trap deactivates and resets after 1 minute. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.445,r=0.513,b=0.481), with ID 5653170a-9877-4630-916e-174800ffef77 -->

SUMMONING RUNE                                    HAZARD 1
COMPLEX  MAGICAL  TRAP
Stealth +7 (trained) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.489,r=0.515,b=0.542), with ID 5bf21357-e012-49a2-9712-89426294a047 -->

**Description** A cloud of invisible magical sensors in a 10-foot radius surrounds an invisible wall or floor rune the size of the creature to be summoned. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.079,r=0.905,b=0.126), with ID 904bee51-bbcd-4339-aad3-0e829b424a3b -->

**Disable** DC 15 Acrobatics to approach without triggering the trap followed by DC 17 Thievery (trained) to erase the rune, or *dispel magic* (1st rank; counteract DC 15) to counteract the rune <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.126,r=0.904,b=0.188), with ID f53e32f9-4f69-4a3a-8c9d-a74a1817333b -->

Summon Monster üîÅ (arcane) Trigger A creature enters the cloud of magical sensors; Effect This trap summons a specific 1st-level creature, determined when the trap is created. The creature rolls initiative and remains for 2d6 rounds, after which the spell ends and the creature disappears. The creature also disappears if someone disables the trap before the duration expires. The summoned creature can use 3 actions each round and can use reactions, unlike most summoned creatures. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.189,r=0.905,b=0.326), with ID d4bec40b-7ce0-432f-bb50-42bba07cb34e -->

**Reset** The trap resets each day at dawn. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.326,r=0.777,b=0.342), with ID 3bc86142-a1e8-4a70-b34d-9cde0f72ef42 -->

# BUILDING HAZARDS

*Building hazards designed for your game allows you to customize them to match your story, location, and needs, as well as to surprise the other players at every turn. There‚Äôs no wrong way to create a hazard, but this guide presents the information in the order you might see it in a hazard stat block.* <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.092,t=0.074,r=0.862,b=0.179), with ID 49e5921f-7ebf-4f86-8723-52cbd74a21aa -->

Concept
The first thing you‚Äôll need is a concept for your hazard. What level is your hazard? Will it be simple or complex? Is it a trap, a haunt, an environmental hazard, or something else? If it‚Äôs a trap, is it mechanical, magical, or both? This is a good time to brainstorm the hazard‚Äôs name and description, as this will help you decide how the hazard can be disabled. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.091,t=0.211,r=0.471,b=0.329), with ID e5d7d72a-e9e4-4341-b366-eaf8ca20c2be -->

## Hazard Types
The three main types of hazards are traps, environmental hazards, and haunts. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.337,r=0.470,b=0.386), with ID 743ec4dd-bba8-491d-b649-24f6e9813f42 -->

Traps are usually built or placed. They can also form
accidentally, such as if a magic portal, through millennia of
disuse, malfunctions as its magic warps. Mechanical traps
always have some physical component, whereas purely
magical traps typically don‚Äôt. Magical traps can usually be
counteracted by *dispel magic*, and those without a listed
proficiency rank for Stealth can be found using *detect magic*.
Thievery is the most common skill used to disable traps. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.388,r=0.471,b=0.509), with ID 8e8e5211-12eb-409a-8017-2afedc8e7301 -->

Environmental hazards are either living things, like
dangerous spores and molds, or simply features of the
terrain or environment, like avalanches or rockslides.
While they are always physical, some environmental
hazards can't reasonably be attacked or damaged, such
as a cloud of poisonous gas or a patch of quicksand.
Survival is the most common skill used to disable
environmental hazards. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.509,r=0.470,b=0.632), with ID 8578d2bd-966a-4f75-985b-22e6c649c918 -->

Haunts are spiritual hazards, usually formed when the spiritual essence of a location is imprinted with the instincts and emotions from a living being‚Äôs demise. Because haunts lack matter, they rarely involve a physical component, and when they do, that component is generally incorporeal or might even be damaged only by vitality energy. The skills and options used to disable haunts vary, though using Religion for an exorcism is common. However, even with a successful check to disable a haunt, it can reoccur until its unfinished business is resolved. Typically, successfully disabling or enduring a haunt provides clues to determine what it would take to lay it to rest permanently. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.633,r=0.470,b=0.832), with ID 1e9923e0-0dc9-4eb2-b357-aa3fefd46529 -->

# UNDERSTANDING AND CHOOSING STATISTICS
Statistics determine how your hazard interacts with the game world, representing how dangerous it is and how hard it is to render harmless or circumvent. The statistics for your hazard <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.841,r=0.458,b=0.940), with ID 67b3b33a-a019-449b-a51b-e6f2e4b571a7 -->

can have extreme, high, or low values. You will want to
choose the value that is most appropriate for the concept
of your hazard, while ensuring that it is well balanced to
ensure a fun encounter. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.486,t=0.219,r=0.863,b=0.280), with ID 2bff071b-dc62-4c75-bb24-4273b6c1d270 -->

**Extreme:** The hazard is world class in this statistic and can challenge almost any character. Almost all hazards have one extreme statistic because hazards normally activate only if they have gone unnoticed or if someone critically failed to disable them. Does it have an extreme Stealth DC that makes it incredibly hard to find, an extreme Disable DC that makes it perilous to disable, or an extreme save DC that makes it deadly in the event it triggers? These are the most common choices, as each affects a different phase of encountering the hazard. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.281,r=0.863,b=0.433), with ID 12361eff-7a33-4c51-8bc3-419dd88dc7f7 -->

**High:** Extremely capable but not world class, the hazard presents a challenge for most characters. This is a capable level, and can generally serve as a baseline value. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.433,r=0.862,b=0.479), with ID 4581a686-0a93-42f5-a526-88be18331d3a -->

**Low:** If a hazard has a weakness, like a poor Reflex save for a bulky mechanical trap or an easy DC to disable <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.479,r=0.862,b=0.511), with ID cd1190cc-a39e-49c6-aee6-adc79cd9985e -->

Summary : This is a color illustration of a kneeling female character, likely a fantasy or role-playing game adventurer, surrounded by tools and equipment on a cobblestone surface.

illustration:

  ‚Ä¢ The main subject is a muscular woman kneeling on cobblestones, holding a metal tool in one hand and a spiked metal ring in the other.
  ‚Ä¢ She wears a sleeveless shirt, dark pants, and bracers on both forearms.
  ‚Ä¢ A belt with pouches, a sheathed dagger, and various tools is strapped around her waist.
  ‚Ä¢ The setting is a top-down or slightly angled perspective, focusing on the character and her immediate surroundings.
  ‚Ä¢ The color palette is earthy, with browns, greys, and muted reds.

Technical Details :
  ‚Ä¢ No scale bar or measurement indicators are present.
  ‚Ä¢ The illustration is detailed, with visible textures on clothing, tools, and the cobblestone ground.
  ‚Ä¢ No on-image text or UI elements.

  ‚Ä¢ Tools and objects are scattered around her: a toolkit with picks, a cog, a screwdriver, a folded cloth, a pouch, and a sheathed dagger.
  ‚Ä¢ The foreground is filled with equipment, while the background is minimal, focusing attention on the subject.

Analysis :
  ‚Ä¢ The image conveys the character‚Äôs preparedness and skill with tools, suggesting a role such as a rogue, tinkerer, or mechanic.

  ‚Ä¢ The illustration emphasizes resourcefulness and readiness, typical of an adventurer archetype. <!-- figure, from page 0 (l=0.463,t=0.522,r=0.878,b=0.972), with ID cb63a05e-3fb6-4e5e-81da-32dd6f9df5cc -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.014,r=0.376,b=0.043), with ID 808627a1-fd69-46c3-bc64-7d25565adc6e -->

for a hard-to-find trap, it usually has a low value. If you  
need something even lower, use a terrible value from  
Building Creatures (pages 112‚Äì125), or just an incredibly  
low value like the *armageddon orb‚Äôs* Stealth (page 100). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.078,r=0.515,b=0.145), with ID 8d640eb8-c7db-45c5-baa8-9e55fea1b483 -->

STEALTH AND DISABLE  
When determining a hazard‚Äôs combat statistics, first decide how the hazard can be located and how hard it is to disable. A hazard where the main challenge is how difficult it is to find, like the hidden pit, might have a very different effect for its level than a hazard out in plain sight, daring a PC to try to disable it, like the armageddon orb. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.153,r=0.515,b=0.281), with ID 48a126de-21e9-4579-80e3-0bbed4c519c4 -->

When deciding how your hazard is disabled, come up
with a narrative description of how it would happen,
which will inform which methods and skills disable
the hazard. You‚Äôll need to decide the proficiency rank
necessary to find the hazard as well as disable it with
each method. Remember, a hazard without a listed
rank next to its Stealth DC is obvious enough that
creatures can find it without Searching, and magical
hazards without a listed rank are not normally protected
against detect magic. Most hazards built by intelligent
creatures are concealed and have at least a trained rank.
The Minimum Proficiency table indicates the high and
moderate proficiency requirements by level; you can use <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.739,r=0.514,b=0.940), with ID 40303fa7-1ada-4b62-8f94-1f5528d3335c -->

lower proficiency ranks than the ones listed, and if you
use the high rank, consider a secondary, perhaps less-
efficient method to disable the hazard using a lower
rank. For instance, the bloodthirsty urge haunt (page
100) can be disabled with master Religion, or by a
higher DC with expert Diplomacy. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.080,r=0.905,b=0.173), with ID 83a143c0-f814-4b12-bc71-8833ee61ea9a -->

If you need a Stealth modifier for a complex hazard,
just subtract 10 from the listed DC. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.173,r=0.904,b=0.204), with ID 0e9401d5-2b46-47ef-9c79-f46705608ab0 -->

DEFENSES  
If there‚Äôs a physical component that a character could break, you‚Äôll need to determine the hazard‚Äôs AC, Fortitude save, and Reflex save, using the extreme, high, and low values (preceded by E, H, or L on the table) as well as its Hardness, HP, and Broken Threshold (BT). When building a purely magical or formless hazard, you can skip this section. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.338,r=0.904,b=0.464), with ID b70353b7-1d10-4ab9-8336-bb396e831d61 -->

* The Broken Threshold is usually half the hazard's HP. <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.475,r=0.902,b=0.939), with ID 6da05beb-9a65-45fd-a19c-d52121ffdad9 -->

110 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.029,t=0.959,r=0.085,b=0.983), with ID 80b21d4c-27f9-42ee-9cbd-c27b72b99ab0 -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.685,t=0.011,r=0.873,b=0.053), with ID b9488ecf-c5e4-4c91-a955-27ca646d1cf8 -->

2 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.905,t=0.010,r=0.981,b=0.056), with ID 9a210ea2-50a4-4f18-b07e-e5351fe10aee -->

Some hazards, even high-level ones, don‚Äôt make sense with a high Hardness value. In those cases, you can skip the Hardness and use the HP values from table 2‚Äì7: Hit Points on page 63. Especially for complex hazards, you might want to divide the durability over multiple sections, located in different positions, to encourage teamwork and mobility. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.090,t=0.078,r=0.475,b=0.191), with ID 38cae22b-fa33-4752-a35a-a2ef7b2c61e3 -->

**OFFENSE**
Almost all hazards need an attack bonus or a save DC, and hazards that deal damage need to list a damage value. Simple hazards deal about twice as much damage as complex hazards and have a very high attack bonus (abbreviated as S. Atk in the table). Complex hazards usually have more moderate attack bonuses (abbreviated as C. Atk in the table). You can adjust them further using the Attack Bonus table on page 120 if your hazard needs it. Simple hazard DCs aren‚Äôt as high for their level as their attack bonuses are, since effects with DCs usually have some effect even on a successful saving throw; use the EDC and HDC columns for extreme and hard DCs in the table. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.090,t=0.197,r=0.474,b=0.418), with ID dba4a189-643b-4111-804f-95157e3a1b60 -->

The damage columns on the table give a damage expression you can use, followed by the average damage in parentheses. If you want to make your own damage expression, remember that average damage is 2.5 for a d4, 3.5 for a d6, 4.5 for a d8, 5.5 for a d10, and 6.5 for a d12. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.090,t=0.418,r=0.474,b=0.497), with ID 4b69db9d-55c5-4b32-a6b4-0f49689e3677 -->

# DESIGNING SIMPLE HAZARDS
When designing a simple hazard, make sure to select an appropriate trigger and effect. Often, a simple hazard that merely damages its target is little more than a speed bump that slows down the game without much added value, so think about the purpose of your hazard carefully, both in the story and in the game world, especially when it‚Äôs a hazard that a creature intentionally built or placed in that location. A great simple hazard does something interesting, has a longer-lasting consequence, or integrates with the nearby inhabitants or even the encounters in some way (you can find more information on integrating hazards with encounters on page 78). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.483,t=0.073,r=0.866,b=0.298), with ID a474b7c2-4106-4dfa-8710-bdb32487bb30 -->

# DESIGNING COMPLEX HAZARDS
Unlike a simple hazard, a complex hazard can play the part of a creature in a battle, or can be an encounter all its own. Many of the concerns with damaging effects when designing a simple hazard don‚Äôt apply when designing a complex hazard. A complex hazard can apply its damage over and over again, eventually killing its hapless victim, and isn‚Äôt intended to be a quick-to-overcome obstacle. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.304,r=0.868,b=0.464), with ID 121d54d1-ffe3-452c-ad21-2bef8e61e3da -->

A good complex hazard often requires disabling
multiple components or otherwise interacting with
the encounter in some way. For instance, while the
poisoned dart gallery (page 107) requires only one
Thievery check to disable, the control panel is on the
far end of the gallery, so a PC would need to make their
way across first. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.483,t=0.464,r=0.868,b=0.575), with ID f373520c-64d8-4885-a2fc-92ba4a38a427 -->

Building Routines  
A complex hazard has a routine each round, whether it stems from preprogrammed instructions built into a trap, instincts and residual emotions swirling around a complex haunt, or a force of nature like sinking in quicksand. Make sure to build a routine that makes sense for the hazard; an environmental lava chute that ejects lava into the area each round shouldn‚Äôt be able to seek out and precisely target only the PCs, but it might spatter random areas within range or everything within range, depending on how you describe the hazard. However, a complex haunt might be able to recognize life force and target living creatures. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.483,t=0.581,r=0.867,b=0.771), with ID 7b5188cc-0999-458e-a2d5-7a92fae61f7d -->

If you create a hazard that can‚Äôt consistently attack
the PCs (like the blade pillar, which moves in a random
direction), you can make it deadlier than normal in
other ways. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.483,t=0.770,r=0.867,b=0.832), with ID 0a9066fd-4cca-4ea9-85c9-ba10ba638a63 -->

The hazard should have as many actions as you feel
it needs to perform its routine. If you split the routine
out into several actions, you can also remove some of
the hazard‚Äôs actions once partial progress is made in
disabling or destroying it; this can give the PCs a feeling
of progress, and it can encourage them to handle the
hazard if it appears in an encounter alongside creatures. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.483,t=0.832,r=0.866,b=0.942), with ID 412ecefa-989a-46e5-bf09-2e54d19320c5 -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.111,t=0.014,r=0.377,b=0.042), with ID 7e4c392e-0656-42a9-a210-9adeac07d4bf -->

# BUILDING CREATURES

*Making your own creatures fleshes out your game world and lets you introduce concepts not yet available in published products like Monster Core and similar volumes. These guidelines help you customize creatures to your specifications and explore your imagination. From strange beasts to canny political rivals, you have the power to design creatures that fit the narrative needs of your story.* <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.135,t=0.077,r=0.909,b=0.200), with ID 38afcfdf-f18c-4a23-9df7-656b2d30699b -->

Creatures aren‚Äôt built the same way PCs are. The rules for
building them are more flexible, and their statistics are
based on benchmark final numbers rather than combining
each individual modifier together. This is called top-down
design, in which you consider the design process as a whole
and select the details that reflect your intended result, rather
than building statistics from the bottom up and hoping the
finished creature matches your vision. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.233,r=0.514,b=0.356), with ID c4016960-a769-4904-9acb-da61871377d1 -->

This guide provides a step-by-step process to build
creatures, but as you get more comfortable with creature
creation, you may prefer to use different methods. You <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.357,r=0.514,b=0.403), with ID a6a0bf46-f624-485e-8a0a-6314457cf2df -->

Summary : This is a fantasy illustration depicting an undead or vampiric elf-like creature wielding a sword, with glowing red eyes and a menacing expression.

illustration:
Scene Overview :
  ‚Ä¢ The main subject is a humanoid creature with pointed ears, long white hair, and grayish skin.
  ‚Ä¢ The creature has glowing red eyes and an open-mouthed snarl, revealing sharp teeth.
  ‚Ä¢ It is draped in a tattered, dark cloak and wears minimal, ragged clothing.
  ‚Ä¢ A large, blood-red gem or wound is visible on its chest.
  ‚Ä¢ The figure is holding a long sword, point-down, with both hands.
  ‚Ä¢ The background is mostly blank, focusing attention on the character.

Technical Details :
  ‚Ä¢ No scale bar, text, or UI elements are present.
  ‚Ä¢ The lighting is dramatic, with highlights on the hair and face, and deep shadows on the cloak and limbs.
  ‚Ä¢ The colour palette is dominated by grays, blacks, and muted greens, with striking red accents in the eyes and chest.

Spatial Relationships :
  ‚Ä¢ The creature is positioned in a dynamic, forward-leaning stance, suggesting aggression or readiness to attack.
  ‚Ä¢ The sword is planted in the ground, anchoring the figure.
  ‚Ä¢ The cloak billows to the left, adding a sense of movement.

Analysis :
  ‚Ä¢ The illustration conveys a sense of menace and supernatural power, emphasizing the creature's undead or vampiric nature through its gaunt features, glowing eyes, and dramatic pose.
  ‚Ä¢ The use of red highlights draws attention to the eyes and chest, reinforcing the creature's otherworldly and dangerous qualities. <!-- figure, from page 0 (l=-0.000,t=0.410,r=0.527,b=0.964), with ID 92334fb8-352c-4cab-bef4-4c348fa7b0f1 -->

could start with one ability you think is cool, or you might
look to create a spellcaster of a certain type. There's no
wrong starting place or wrong way to compile and present
your creation; some GMs prefer to generate a stat block
that is as similar to an official *Monster Core* entry as
possible, while others prefer just a brief set of notes. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.235,r=0.904,b=0.327), with ID 5c7aa30d-1c8e-4441-b253-3276572c5357 -->

DEVELOP THE CONCEPT  
To begin, come up with the creature‚Äôs concept. You likely already have the basic idea. As you add details to the general idea, taking notes can help keep your creature on track. Consider the parts of your creature you find most compelling and want to emphasize when the creature hits the table. For example, in Monster Core, demons are creatures of sin and are designed to have weaknesses against specific virtues that oppose them. Satyrs enchant creatures by playing their pipes, represented by their centerpiece ability, Play the Pipes. Note your creature‚Äôs core aspects, and if you feel uncertain later, you can look back and ask yourself, ‚ÄúDoes this emphasize a core aspect or not?‚Äù <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.337,r=0.905,b=0.539), with ID 6b185ee2-0841-420d-9110-450f6e3fcf5f -->

Next, look at the creature‚Äôs role in your game. Is it
meant to be a combatant? A social creature? A trusted
ally? Figuring this out will help you determine whether to
give it strong combat abilities or to focus on skills, spells,
and special abilities. Think about how the creature might
behave if it‚Äôs in a fight, if someone tries to talk to it, or if
it‚Äôs in a social situation. Does it work better alone or with
allies? What sort of character should be best at facing it or
be particularly weak against it? <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.541,r=0.904,b=0.677), with ID aec35624-76a8-4bcd-891e-fd089eed78e2 -->

Also consider the complexity of the creature. This
matters most when you plan to use a large number of
creatures of that type. If you‚Äôll use five at the same time,
you‚Äôll want their turns to move swiftly and avoid complex
special actions. A creature that is likely to face a group of
PCs alone can have more abilities, and it might need a more
versatile set of defenses against PC tactics. Cut complexity
as much as you can while retaining your desired theme. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.679,r=0.903,b=0.800), with ID 766167aa-c9ad-4d05-913f-e1423d78d909 -->

Now, how do you want an encounter with this creature to
feel? Should it be scary? Mobile? Confusing? A mystical
duel or a knock-down, drag-out fight? What can you give
your creature to convey those characteristics? Much of this
feel will come from your choice of the creature‚Äôs special
abilities or spells, rather than its raw numbers. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.532,t=0.802,r=0.902,b=0.892), with ID 424529e0-1d6c-4bf6-9574-e4cf8deee837 -->

With all this in mind, think about what specific abilities
your creature should have. Take a few notes now, and
get to the details later. You can use abilities from <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.535,t=0.893,r=0.902,b=0.938), with ID 02100212-212c-4e63-9fe9-ac606fa01376 -->

112 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.001,t=0.959,r=0.084,b=0.982), with ID fd051e56-1e33-4c5c-94d4-d5404ad9f4ed -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.686,t=0.014,r=0.874,b=0.048), with ID 4a481036-81c3-4de9-9d90-174b0535e840 -->

2 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.908,t=0.014,r=0.975,b=0.054), with ID fe60d029-e330-4174-961d-41e75a7ab9c6 -->

*Monster Core* or feats in *Player Core*, adjusting as needed, to save yourself time. It helps to think of a creature that‚Äôs similar to yours and see what makes it tick‚Äîand what you can steal from it. Maybe you can just reskin that creature (page 114), instead of making a new one from scratch. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.079,r=0.473,b=0.159), with ID 481df69f-0e04-4f57-bfcb-72be3c2f1923 -->

Now that you understand your creature‚Äôs concept, it‚Äôs
time to get to the statistics. Remember that you can always
change your concept later on. Your creation might evolve
and transform as you go, so be open to change. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.159,r=0.473,b=0.221), with ID a8e841e5-d1e7-42a9-9ef2-adf0aaa81941 -->

# UNDERSTANDING STATISTICS
Most of the statistics in this section use a scale of extreme,
high, moderate, and low‚Äîsome use terrible values as well.

**Extreme:** The creature is world-class in this statistic and
can challenge almost any character. Most creatures have no
extreme statistics or only one extreme statistic, although
some creatures might have additional extreme statistics
and weaker related statistics elsewhere (a common example
being a creature trading accuracy for extreme damage).
Examples from *Monster Core* include the succubus‚Äôs
Diplomacy modifier and the lich‚Äôs spell DC. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.228,r=0.473,b=0.402), with ID 6042aaa5-9101-414e-b457-ca5d353a3cb8 -->

**High:** Extremely capable but not world-class, the creature presents a challenge for most characters. Just about all creatures have at least one high value. Most combat-focused creatures have high AC and either a high attack bonus and high damage, or a merely moderate attack bonus but extreme damage. An ogre warrior‚Äôs attack bonus and a kobold scout‚Äôs Stealth are high values. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.402,r=0.473,b=0.511), with ID b56b46a5-5279-4cf8-be88-914543ba9d44 -->

**Moderate:** A middle-of-the road statistic covers anything unremarkable about the creature. Use this one often. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.511,r=0.473,b=0.541), with ID a7c6f113-53d3-4485-b9cb-9139247ee74c -->

**Low:** The creature is actively bad at this. Choose these intentionally to represent the creature‚Äôs weak points. Most creatures should have at least one low statistic; an example is the goblin pyro‚Äôs Will save. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.541,r=0.472,b=0.602), with ID ade875dd-d2a2-40d1-aad3-88e6d12e154b -->

**Terrible:** Some statistics can dip even lower than low, to terrible. This indicates a truly awful statistic that still isn‚Äôt game-breakingly bad. A spider‚Äôs Intelligence is terrible, as is an ogre‚Äôs Will save. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.602,r=0.472,b=0.664), with ID 20065f39-aa7d-4df5-bd26-e46a60f1dd6f -->

Push and Pull  
Statistics should be balanced overall. That means if you‚Äôre giving a creature an extreme statistic, it should have some low or terrible statistics to compensate. For example, if you were making a creature extremely hard to hit by giving it an extreme AC, you‚Äôd likely give it lower saving throws or low HP. If a creature is great at spellcasting, it might need several low statistics to be a balanced challenge. There‚Äôs no perfect system for making these decisions. If you‚Äôve made a creature that has four high stats and nothing low, or vice-versa, take another look. A creature‚Äôs strengths and weaknesses change the PCs‚Äô strategies for dealing with it, and that‚Äôs what makes playing the game fun! <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.672,r=0.473,b=0.880), with ID e12eb1b9-e905-4780-9e6c-8ed43f1b6e89 -->

**Extreme Increases**
At the higher levels of the game, PCs have more tools at their disposal, so the creatures they face need to hit back <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.887,r=0.474,b=0.943), with ID 51150bc2-920d-4df0-809b-92bf37041156 -->

BUILDING CREATURES OVERVIEW
This section details the creature-building process using the following steps.

1. Develop the Concept (page 112) Think about your creature, and make notes you can use in future steps.
2. Build the Stat Block Pick all the statistics for the creatures, going through the list below.
   Level (page 114)
   Size and Traits (page 114)
   Attribute Modifiers (page 114)
   Perception and Senses (page 115)
   Languages (page 116)
   Skills (page 116)
   Items, if necessary (page 116)
   AC (page 117)
   Saving Throws (page 118)
   Hit Points (page 118)
   Immunities, Weaknesses, and Resistances (page 119)
   Speed (page 119)
   Strikes, including their damage (page 119)
   Spells, if necessary (page 121)
3. Design Abilities (page 123) Create the special abilities your creature can use.
4. Review Holistically (page 125) Step back, take stock of your creature as a whole, and tweak as needed. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.489,t=0.075,r=0.858,b=0.484), with ID 965533c9-184e-4b0b-976f-3ca824914804 -->

TRAIT ABILITIES (PAGE 126)
This section provides the abilities conveyed by certain
traits, such as demon, dragon, and undead. You'll also
find abilities typical of creatures with those traits to help
guide you as you plan your creatures. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.491,t=0.487,r=0.856,b=0.575), with ID 8b1e8b2b-9019-4176-985e-74b624413866 -->

## DESIGNING NPCS (PAGE 128)
Sometimes you‚Äôll design a creature that‚Äôs meant to have abilities or characteristics similar to those of a PC. Maybe you need a bold champion, a sly rogue master criminal, or a wizened druid elder for your game. You also might need a common baker, who has little combat ability but great skill with dough and an oven. This section provides ways you might modify aspects of the creature-building process to fit those needs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.494,t=0.578,r=0.855,b=0.726), with ID 79d82759-da90-4a90-8cc3-101679b1c0a6 -->

harder! At higher levels, give each creature more extreme
statistics. Having one extreme statistic becomes typical
around 11th level. A creature of 15th level or higher
typically has two extreme statistics, and one of 20th level
or higher should have three or four. Keep in mind that
these should be relevant to the encounters you expect them
to have‚Äîextreme social skills aren‚Äôt much use to a combat-
focused creature. Be careful about giving multiple extreme
statistics that are closely linked: a creature with extreme
damage and Fortitude saves is one thing, but having an
extreme attack bonus and extreme damage allows the
creature to apply both extreme statistics to each attack. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.485,t=0.752,r=0.864,b=0.942), with ID 1dc7c769-434b-4d9b-a924-a75a735a00e8 -->

# RESKINNING A CREATURE
Sometimes you need a creature with abilities that are almost exactly the same as those of a published creature. In that case, it can be more efficient to simply ‚Äúreskin‚Äù the old creature rather than design a new one‚Äîthat is, to change the description but keep the abilities mostly the same. Occasionally, reskinning requires small mechanical adjustments. For instance, a fire cat that has immunity to fire, an aura that deals fire damage, and the ability to light people on fire with its jaws to deal persistent fire damage could be reskinned as a caustic animate tree that has immunity to acid, an aura that deals acid damage, and branch attacks that smear acidic sap on a creature‚Äôs body when they hit, dealing persistent acid damage. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.074,r=0.513,b=0.325), with ID 6db45b2f-98ed-479d-9183-87e62da4c227 -->

LEVEL
For most creatures you build, their level depends on the level of the party that will encounter it. Look at other creatures you think are similar in power to yours to determine its level. Note that level represents a creature‚Äôs combat ability, so a creature that‚Äôs more social might have 3rd-level combat statistics and 6th-level skills, but it would remain a 3rd-level creature. Most such creatures are NPCs; for more information on this distinction and how to use it, see Non-Combat Level on page 128. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.336,r=0.515,b=0.509), with ID aaa088f2-c561-4c5e-b02f-3b3d55217a6e -->

Some abilities are hard for PCs to deal with at low
levels. For instance, creatures that can fly and have
ranged attacks should typically appear around 7th level,
when PCs gain access to flight. Natural invisibility or at-
will *invisibility* as an innate spell should come at around
6th level, when PCs are more likely to prepare *see the
unseen* in lower-rank spell slots, or 8th level, when some
PCs get the Blind-Fight feat. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.510,r=0.514,b=0.632), with ID 9c2eb6d9-fa8c-4a36-a6f4-227ac4e05699 -->

The tables in this chapter go up to 24th level‚Äîthe
highest-level extreme encounter a party might face. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.632,r=0.513,b=0.664), with ID d10fbbd4-0df9-42f8-bab0-9c9b721d1556 -->

## SIZE AND TRAITS
Fill out the trait line of your creature‚Äôs stat block. Creatures can be whatever size you need them to be, though you seldom find Large creatures below 1st level, Huge creatures below 5th level, or Gargantuan creatures below 10th level. Generally, you don‚Äôt automatically adjust statistics for size, except to Strength modifiers for Large and bigger creatures, which you‚Äôll find in the following Attribute Modifiers section. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.674,r=0.513,b=0.816), with ID 767b66dc-b184-49c4-9b5b-46e3d82cebbd -->

Your creature will almost certainly have one of the following traits to define its type: aberration, animal, astral, beast, celestial, construct, dragon, elemental, ethereal, fey, fiend, fungus, giant, humanoid, monitor, ooze, plant, or undead. If you‚Äôre making a creature from an existing category of a type, such as demon, it also has that category as a trait. Creatures with a close affinity to elements‚Äîair, earth, fire, metal, water and wood‚Äîor <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.817,r=0.513,b=0.939), with ID ce8a5d0a-d6ec-439c-97aa-c650e2dea80f -->

types of energy‚Äîlike acid, cold, and electricity‚Äîhave  
those traits. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.080,r=0.904,b=0.111), with ID d384f06d-2b6e-4ed4-89d3-d8f246a906ce -->

Some abilities that are typical of creatures with the
traits listed here can be found in Trait Abilities on page
126. As with the other steps, looking at similar creatures
will give you an idea of what traits to use. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.112,r=0.904,b=0.172), with ID 9045a152-c7f2-45d4-a6a0-6b9810528081 -->

Add any traits that have detailed rules attached to
them, like amphibious, aquatic, incorporeal, mindless,
and swarm. You can add traits related to the creature
category, such as dinosaur or werecreature, but most
of these traits are pretty self-evident in play. If at any
point you realize during play that you didn‚Äôt add a trait
the creature really should have, you can usually apply
it retroactively. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.173,r=0.904,b=0.295), with ID 06cf1507-33e9-4e96-8495-f3c46e35ce57 -->

## ATTRIBUTE MODIFIERS
Next, figure out your creature‚Äôs attribute modifiers, since these will suggest what their other statistics should be. You don‚Äôt have to determine the exact numbers, but it‚Äôs good to avoid creating creatures whose attribute modifiers are at odds with their abilities, like creatures with a terrible Wisdom modifier and a very high Perception. Most of the time, you‚Äôll just be using attribute modifiers for untrained skills, so they‚Äôre useful as a guide but not crucial. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.307,r=0.904,b=0.463), with ID 6f0db3ba-9831-4e50-8918-d2c42edaec75 -->

The Attribute Modifier Scales table shows some benchmarks for your creatures. Use high for the creature‚Äôs best attribute modifiers, moderate for ones they‚Äôre okay at, and low for the rest. If a creature has a truly bad ability, you can go as low as ‚Äì5. That‚Äôs the terrible range for attribute modifiers, and doesn‚Äôt really change by level. This is most common with animals, which have an Intelligence modifier of ‚Äì4 (for dogs, dolphins, and such) or ‚Äì5 (for more instinctual animals like spiders), and for mindless creatures, which have a ‚Äì5 Intelligence modifier. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.465,r=0.903,b=0.631), with ID 72082cbb-94c7-405a-a827-2fb729d5c84d -->

Few creatures use the extreme column. A powerful,
dedicated spellcaster might use an extreme spellcasting
statistic, or a preternaturally charming creature like a
succubus or nymph might have an extreme Charisma
modifier. However, the most common way extreme
numbers are used is for really big, really strong creatures.
This happens with only Large or bigger creatures from
1st to 5th level, Huge or larger creatures from 6th to 9th
level, and Gargantuan creatures from 10th to 15th level.
Beyond that level, a creature doesn‚Äôt gain an extreme
Strength modifier from size alone. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.634,r=0.903,b=0.800), with ID 08096788-1546-411b-8475-7fabdeee27a9 -->

Perception

Perception is a fairly straightforward statistic. Use Wisdom as a guide for setting it, and adjust to the high side if your creature has acute senses or extra training. If your creature has low Wisdom, for example, it would probably have a low Perception modifier, or moderate if it‚Äôs supposed to be a great hunter. Don‚Äôt make your creature‚Äôs Perception higher just because it‚Äôs often used for initiative; creatures with poor Perception could use a skill check for initiative instead, such as Stealth. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.398,r=0.472,b=0.558), with ID ac01452f-3df3-4c15-892d-64a6466a61f0 -->

Senses  
Choose or design any special senses for your creature, such as low-light vision, darkvision, or scent. If you‚Äôre making a sense from scratch, simply decide what it senses, whether it has a range limit, and whether it‚Äôs precise or imprecise. For example, a sinspawn has ‚Äúsin scent (imprecise) 30 feet.‚Äù This means it can smell creatures bearing its associated sin if they‚Äôre within 30 feet, and the sense is imprecise‚Äîabout as acute as human hearing. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.567,r=0.471,b=0.711), with ID 3ae14408-f84a-4926-a501-eadea084db5c -->

PERCEPTION
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Extreme</th><th>High</th><th>Moderate</th><th>Low</th><th>Terrible</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>-1</td><td>+9</td><td>+8</td><td>+5</td><td>+2</td><td>+0</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>+10</td><td>+9</td><td>+6</td><td>+3</td><td>+1</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>+11</td><td>+10</td><td>+7</td><td>+4</td><td>+2</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>+12</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td><td>+5</td><td>+3</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>+14</td><td>+12</td><td>+9</td><td>+6</td><td>+4</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>+15</td><td>+14</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td><td>+6</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>+17</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td><td>+9</td><td>+7</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>+18</td><td>+17</td><td>+14</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>+20</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td><td>+10</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>+21</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td><td>+13</td><td>+11</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>+23</td><td>+21</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>+24</td><td>+22</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td><td>+14</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.720,r=0.469,b=0.941), with ID 680bae41-4592-4b52-8c21-fa3daac6d5be -->

## BASE ROAD MAPS
You can use the following suggestions to set the baseline when creating your road map. For example, use the brute for a big, tough creature like an ogre, and the skirmisher for a darting enemy. Each entry is a starting point you can customize as you see fit. Any core statistic that isn‚Äôt listed should typically use moderate numbers. You can set attribute modifiers and add additional abilities as needed. To make a creature that resembles a character of a certain class, see Class Road Maps on page 129. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.498,t=0.085,r=0.851,b=0.248), with ID 9b617127-1d2d-4369-9ddc-acc68ccea3cd -->

**Brute** low Perception; high or extreme Str modifier, high to moderate Con modifier, low or lower Dex and mental modifiers; moderate or low AC; high Fortitude, low Reflex or Will or both; high HP; high attack bonus and high damage or moderate attack bonus and extreme damage <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.499,t=0.249,r=0.849,b=0.341), with ID 6e230dc0-e033-42c9-8243-c754d29d20c2 -->

**Magical Striker** high attack and high damage; moderate to high spell DCs; either a scattering of innate spells, or prepared or spontaneous spells with a rank up to half the creature‚Äôs level (rounded up) minus 1 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.499,t=0.341,r=0.848,b=0.402), with ID 2f272b3f-6a4a-404c-8a63-5883e3b270ac -->

**Skill Paragon** high or extreme attribute modifier matching its best skills; typically high Reflex or Will and low Fortitude; many skills at moderate or high and potentially one or two extreme skills; at least one special ability to use the creature‚Äôs skills in combat <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.499,t=0.402,r=0.847,b=0.479), with ID 364bab84-8c11-4716-8124-36109ccf7a63 -->

**Skirmisher** high Dex modifier; low Fortitude, high Reflex; higher Speed than typical <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.499,t=0.478,r=0.847,b=0.510), with ID 1c5f312a-f611-4f91-a36d-8ceaf5b1903b -->

Sniper high Perception; high Dex modifier; low Fortitude, high Reflex; moderate to low HP; ranged Strikes have high attack bonus and damage or moderate attack bonus and extreme damage (melee Strikes are weaker) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.501,t=0.510,r=0.847,b=0.571), with ID 8b27ba82-3243-4c0c-bad2-5faffc66299c -->

**Soldier** high Str modifier; high to extreme AC; high Fortitude; high attack bonus and high damage; Reactive Strike or other tactical abilities <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.500,t=0.571,r=0.849,b=0.617), with ID 32e48e16-b361-4a01-8bef-305090427c91 -->

**Spellcaster** high or extreme modifier for the spellcasting attribute; low Fortitude, high Will; low HP; low attack bonus and moderate or low damage; high or extreme spell DCs; prepared or spontaneous spells with a rank of up to half the creature‚Äôs level (rounded up) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.501,t=0.617,r=0.849,b=0.695), with ID 8c316c9b-9508-4f72-acae-329091743cc7 -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.111,t=0.014,r=0.377,b=0.042), with ID aaa0e18e-8cb4-429a-9776-87477a98d7d6 -->

## CONVERTING FIRST EDITION CREATURES
If you're converting creatures from First Edition, you won't find a direct numerical conversion. Instead, use the original statistics to create your road map, giving a better AC to a creature that had a good AC in First Edition, and so on.
    Here are the main areas of difference that you'll want to keep in mind for your conversion. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.148,t=0.085,r=0.500,b=0.202), with ID 292d8c79-74a9-474d-9cfb-7edab115a190 -->

- ‚Ä¢ Attribute modifiers scale differently, so don't copy them exactly. The highest modifiers tend not to get as high in Second Edition. You'll rarely see a +10 Strength modifier, for example. Creatures also tend to get better low statistics at higher levels than they used to, particularly for Dexterity and Wisdom. This is most apparent in high-level First Edition creatures with awful Dexterity.
- ‚Ä¢ Low-Intelligence creatures, particularly animals, tend to have more special actions than they would have in First Edition. This is to make encounters with them more dynamic and distinct. Compare dinosaurs between the editions for good examples. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.149,t=0.203,r=0.500,b=0.387), with ID 06685a46-5302-4dac-83a2-371446495de1 -->

‚Ä¢ When converting spell-like abilities to innate spells, you might need to make some substitutions. Some spells will appear as heightened versions of spells (such as greater dispel magic now being heightened dispel magic), but others will require you to find something different. Also, don‚Äôt feel like you need to keep every spell; focus on the most thematic and potent ones. The Spells section on page 121 has more advice on this subject. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.150,t=0.387,r=0.500,b=0.510), with ID 942ea665-bca5-4e1d-aecd-507d58cf3a28 -->

- ‚Ä¢ Damage reduction has been replaced with two options: resistance to all damage (possibly with exceptions), or more HP and a weakness. Immunities, Weaknesses, and Resistances on page 119 describes the distinction.
- ‚Ä¢ If you want to convert spell resistance, you can give the creature a +1 status bonus to all saves against magic, or +2 if it had abnormally high spell resistance for its level. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.155,t=0.511,r=0.500,b=0.617), with ID 43c95445-4485-49da-9de4-adf57989f368 -->

# Languages
Think about what languages the creature would need to communicate with other creatures in its home. For instance, many intelligent undead speak Necril, and many creatures from the Darklands speak Sakvroth. If you want your creature to be able to speak to the PCs, be sure it has Common; for a creature with no reason to speak the common tongue of your setting (such as most extraplanar creatures in a typical campaign), be sure it doesn‚Äôt. Some creatures can understand language but can‚Äôt vocalize; in this case, you can state that they can‚Äôt speak any language. For creatures that need to be able to infiltrate and communicate wherever they go, you might give them truespeech or a similar ability as a constant innate spell. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.645,r=0.513,b=0.863), with ID 9120bf23-bc9c-467b-aece-32691bf2cdfe -->

Skills
You have lots of flexibility in setting your creature‚Äôs skills.
Pick some skills you think are appropriate, and consider
how good the creature is at them. High skills are roughly <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.874,r=0.512,b=0.941), with ID 3486cc11-f216-427f-b0b6-07507fa7cd09 -->

on par with a specialized PC of the creature‚Äôs level, though
they could be a little lower or higher. Most creatures have
at least one high skill, but no more than three. The best
skills should go with the best attribute modifiers, and you
might even want to estimate the creature‚Äôs proficiency
rank for these skills. Some skills can get a high bonus for
free to fit the creature‚Äôs theme, particularly Lore skills. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.081,r=0.904,b=0.187), with ID f84ee2b8-0329-4ac0-9b36-14206b1cbfc8 -->

Most creatures don‚Äôt have an extreme skill unless they are world-class for their level, like a succubus‚Äôs Diplomacy. Having an extreme skill is less impactful than having an extreme AC or attack bonus, but it still might warrant a sacrifice elsewhere, especially if the creature also has more high skills than usual. There‚Äôs no need for terrible skill modifiers, since an untrained skill usually represents that. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.189,r=0.903,b=0.295), with ID f2e20d96-8323-4904-8e17-2a91e95317f3 -->

+32</td></tr><tr><td>24</td><td>+48</td><td>+45</td><td>+40</td><td>+38 to +33</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.308,r=0.902,b=0.741), with ID 5f9265ec-f4bd-470c-8071-e5b1efecd346 -->

**Special Modifiers**  
You can also add special, thematic modifiers for certain skill uses. For instance, you might give a creature that secretes adhesive ‚ÄúAthletics +7 (+9 to Climb or Grab).‚Äù This special bonus should still remain at or below the extreme number, especially if it has a combat purpose like the Grab bonus above. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.751,r=0.904,b=0.863), with ID 799ed61e-34e6-4de8-ab62-cc3535f1e8e7 -->

Items
If you gave a creature gear equivalent to a PC, your PCs would gain a huge amount of treasure by defeating a large group of them. Using the Safe Items table can help you <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.875,r=0.904,b=0.940), with ID ff6f6908-2c3b-4aec-9234-3d51765e8409 -->

116 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.029,t=0.959,r=0.084,b=0.983), with ID 43348136-0086-4577-b4ee-4fbf453f7ceb -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.690,t=0.019,r=0.867,b=0.042), with ID b148bb00-3dfa-41f6-960c-34848d8d3e25 -->

avoid that. A creature can have a single permanent item
of the listed level without issue. For example, if a 6th-level
creature has a +1 weapon, that item‚Äôs not worth enough that
the PCs would be massively rich if they encountered many
creatures of that type and sold everything they found. You
can give a creature several lower-level items too. Just pay
attention to your overall treasure as measured against the
Safe Item table below. At the lowest levels, a creature can
certainly have multiple level 0 items, even though normally
a creature should have only one item of the level listed in
the Safe Item Level column. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.079,r=0.471,b=0.248), with ID da293d65-597c-45e7-ae84-07737fe925ef -->

Specific creatures or NPCs have more leeway to break
these guidelines because you can plan the rest of your
adventure‚Äôs loot around them. Also, giving a boss a
powerful magic item makes the fight and its aftermath
more interesting. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.249,r=0.471,b=0.326), with ID 3ab3d735-51a8-4fd6-a731-f5e75b9e3969 -->

## DEFENSES
Consider adjusting your creature‚Äôs HP, AC, and saves in tandem based on its theme. Almost no creature has great defenses in all areas, and such creatures often result in frustrating fights. A creature with extreme AC might mean reducing its HP to the next lowest category, or reducing its HP by a smaller amount and making another reduction elsewhere. On the other hand, a creature that‚Äôs easy to hit could have more HP and a strong Fortitude save to compensate. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.097,t=0.705,r=0.470,b=0.863), with ID b4a4d267-bfc7-4061-8c0f-5f44f3c5e769 -->

Armor Class  
Because AC is one of the most important combat stats, you need to be more careful when setting this number for any creature you expect to end up in a fight. Low AC <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.874,r=0.470,b=0.941), with ID 54f80281-f378-44fa-a307-40fa0452a522 -->

IMPROVISING A CREATURE
As you get more experienced, you might find that you don‚Äôt need to build some creatures in advance. In many cases, especially for simple creatures, you can just select values from the relevant tables on the fly and track its HP. When you do, track which value you used as it came up. For instance, let‚Äôs say you‚Äôre improvising a 2nd-level kobold soldier. When it‚Äôs time for initiative, you decide it has moderate Perception and jot down ‚ÄúPer +8.‚Äù Your group‚Äôs fighter beats it at initiative and attacks. You decide the soldier has high AC‚Äîlooking at the Armor Class table, you see that‚Äôs 18‚Äîand add this information to your note. The fighter‚Äôs Strike hits, and you select the low end of high HP: 36. Well, now it‚Äôs 25. Your note says ‚ÄúPer +8, AC 18, HP 25.‚Äù If it gets to take a turn, you can give it a Strike then. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.077,r=0.859,b=0.353), with ID 54a870ee-b24e-43b0-a82c-ba31f414514a -->

typically fits spellcasters, who compensate with their
selection of powerful spells. Most creatures use high or
moderate AC‚Äîhigh is comparable to what a PC fighter
would have. Reserve extreme AC for a creature that is
even better defended; these values are for creatures that
have defenses similar in power to those of a champion
or monk. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.372,r=0.861,b=0.479), with ID 456a3a8f-6933-4313-95cc-e57d08a1fb8e -->

ARMOR CLASS

Saving Throws  
You can often set saves quickly by assigning one high, one moderate, and one low modifier. Some creatures might vary from this, either because they have poor AC but better saves or because they should thematically have multiple good saves and compensate elsewhere. You have more flexibility with saves, and having one save be 1 more or 1 less than the listed number is rarely a big deal. Pay attention to the creature‚Äôs Con, Dex, and Wis modifiers‚Äîthese don‚Äôt have to correspond to the creature‚Äôs saves exactly, but should inform your choices. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.075,r=0.516,b=0.249), with ID fd410bf0-0832-4759-9f2e-925ef7f83ea9 -->

Extreme saves often pair with extreme or high
attribute modifiers. Almost no creature should have
more than one extreme save, even at high levels. Assign
terrible saves to creatures that have a clear weak point‚Äî
for example, a nearly immobile creature would have a
terrible Reflex save. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.250,r=0.515,b=0.343), with ID 9d487900-a2f4-4c82-8152-0ad049648e69 -->

SAVING THROWS
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Extreme</th><th>High</th><th>Moderate</th><th>Low</th><th>Terrible</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>-1</td><td>+9</td><td>+8</td><td>+5</td><td>+2</td><td>+0</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>+10</td><td>+9</td><td>+6</td><td>+3</td><td>+1</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>+11</td><td>+10</td><td>+7</td><td>+4</td><td>+2</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>+12</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td><td>+5</td><td>+3</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>+14</td><td>+12</td><td>+9</td><td>+6</td><td>+4</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>+15</td><td>+14</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td><td>+6</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>+17</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td><td>+9</td><td>+7</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>+18</td><td>+17</td><td>+14</td><td>+11</td><td>+8</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>+20</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td><td>+10</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>+21</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td><td>+13</td><td>+11</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>+23</td><td>+21</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>+24</td><td>+22</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td><td>+14</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>+26</td><td>+24</td><td>+21</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>+27</td><td>+25</td><td>+22</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>+29</td><td>+26</td><td>+23</td><td>+20</td><td>+18</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>+30</td><td>+28</td><td>+25</td><td>+22</td><td>+19</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>+32</td><td>+29</td><td>+26</td><td>+23</td><td>+20</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>+33</td><td>+30</td><td>+28</td><td>+25</td><td>+22</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>+35</td><td>+32</td><td>+29</td><td>+26</td><td>+23</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>+36</td><td>+33</td><td>+30</td><td>+27</td><td>+24</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>+38</td><td>+35</td><td>+32</td><td>+29</td><td>+26</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>+39</td><td>+36</td><td>+33</td><td>+30</td><td>+27</td></tr><tr><td>21</td><td>+41</td><td>+38</td><td>+35</td><td>+32</td><td>+28</td></tr><tr><td>22</td><td>+43</td><td>+39</td><td>+36</td><td>+33</td><td>+30</td></tr><tr><td>23</td><td>+44</td><td>+40</td><td>+37</td><td>+34</td><td>+31</td></tr><tr><td>24</td><td>+46</td><td>+42</td><td>+38</td><td>+36</td><td>+32</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.352,r=0.513,b=0.789), with ID 24303c7d-9e8d-4ee1-88fe-bd8eac858227 -->

Hit Points
Give a creature HP in the moderate range unless its theme strongly suggests it should use another range. Spellcasters, for example, often have low HP. Brutish creatures usually have high HP, compensating with lower AC, weaker saves, fewer tactical options, or other limitations. As mentioned in the Defenses section above, you don‚Äôt want a creature with extreme AC to have high HP too. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.796,r=0.515,b=0.940), with ID 50386b5b-23a8-44c3-8ddf-3ad41334fe3b -->

Hit Points are closely tied in with immunities, weaknesses,
and resistances, so if your creature has any of those, look at
that section before finalizing HP (page 119). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.080,r=0.904,b=0.128), with ID d1c62963-60b0-40d5-8baa-6baac9354f43 -->

Regeneration and Healing Abilities  
Your creature might have regeneration, fast healing, or some other ability to heal itself. These healing abilities can greatly affect the flow of a fight. Regeneration or fast healing heals a number of hits each round‚Äîusually one to one and a half hits. To determine the number of Hit Points it should restore, look at the high damage value on the Strike Damage table (page 120) and multiply that value by the number of hits healed. For instance, if the high damage is 20, regeneration between 20 to 30 makes sense. The value should be higher if the regeneration is easy to overcome‚Äîand remember that most regeneration gets easier to overcome at higher levels. Also, you might want to decrease the creature‚Äôs total HP by double its regeneration value. Fast healing follows the same rules, but because it can‚Äôt prevent a creature‚Äôs death and there isn‚Äôt always a way to deactivate it, you might want to give the creature more HP instead of fast healing to keep things simple. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.138,r=0.905,b=0.432), with ID ee352bc7-bd24-4362-9035-9ac1f7942ad8 -->

If a creature can use an ability that heals it, that ability
typically restores more HP since it costs actions. An at-
will healing ability should be based on a heal spell 2
ranks lower than the highest-rank spell a creature of
that level could ordinarily cast (for example, an 11th-
level creature can typically cast up to 6th-rank spells,
so you would base its healing ability on a 4th-rank heal
spell). If the ability both deals damage and heals, use
that same baseline scale from above but with vampiric
feast instead of heal. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.434,r=0.905,b=0.587), with ID 33d3213e-9120-4bd4-be18-26941ad2c799 -->

## IMMUNITIES, WEAKNESSES, AND RESISTANCES
If it‚Äôs highly thematic for a creature to have an immunity, weakness, or resistance, consider adding it. The Resistances and Weaknesses table lists the ranges for weaknesses and resistances by level. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.183,r=0.473,b=0.280), with ID ec43db12-f56f-4ec8-9a91-6d79ade477bd -->

Immunities are generally reserved for creatures made
of an unusual substance (like a fire elemental being
immune to fire). You can also give an immunity if a
creature‚Äôs biology or construction would logically cause
it to be unaffected (like a mindless creature‚Äôs immunity
to mental effects). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.280,r=0.471,b=0.371), with ID 55d9a187-2a4a-4440-9870-af3ef34d7dfa -->

If the creature should be hard to affect with something but the conditions above aren‚Äôt true, give it a resistance instead. For instance, a giant octopus isn‚Äôt actually made of cold water, so it wouldn‚Äôt be immune to cold, but its life in the ocean depths makes it resistant to cold. You‚Äôll typically use the lower end of the value on the Resistances and Weaknesses table for a broad resistance that applies to a wide range of effects, like ‚Äúphysical 5 (except silver)‚Äù and the higher end for something narrower, like a single damage type. A creature with a resistance, especially a broad resistance or a physical resistance, usually has fewer HP. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.373,r=0.471,b=0.555), with ID 34f896a7-57c7-45be-9f2d-6ab3b8cdd35d -->

Giving your creature a weakness adds flavor to it
and greatly rewards effective player tactics once your
players identify the weakness. The weakness should
apply to one damage type or phenomenon and use the
high end of the scale. Creatures typically have at most
one weakness. If a creature has a weakness, especially
to something common, give it additional HP. The
amount of additional HP might depend on how tough
the creature should feel if the PCs don‚Äôt exploit its
weakness; a tough creature might have additional HP
equal to quadruple the weakness value. A creature with
a hard-to-exploit weakness might have additional HP
equal to the weakness value or less. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.557,r=0.471,b=0.755), with ID 87f70a50-13ed-438b-a18a-2f6768f85df6 -->

RESISTANCES AND WEAKNESSES
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Maximum</th><th>Minimum</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>-1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>3</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>5</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>6</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>7</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>8</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>9</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>10</td><td>5</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.766,r=0.469,b=0.941), with ID 288e2586-b691-4286-b942-34a188ac8407 -->

<table><tbody><tr><td>8</td><td>11</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>12</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>13</td><td>7</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>14</td><td>7</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>15</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>16</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>17</td><td>9</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>18</td><td>9</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>19</td><td>9</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>19</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>20</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>21</td><td>11</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>22</td><td>11</td></tr><tr><td>21</td><td>23</td><td>12</td></tr><tr><td>22</td><td>24</td><td>12</td></tr><tr><td>23</td><td>25</td><td>13</td></tr><tr><td>24</td><td>26</td><td>13</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.078,r=0.860,b=0.345), with ID db06e5f6-d559-4d2c-867d-3efa1b70cfc8 -->

The combination of more HP and a weakness has a different feel from standard HP with resistances. If the creature being an impervious tank really fits its theme, use a resistance with an exception, such as ‚Äúphysical 5 (except silver).‚Äù If, however, it makes more sense for normal hits to get through and the creature to simply have great staying power, use more HP and a weakness. Skeletons and zombies are a good example of the difference between these styles. Skeletons have resistances because they‚Äôre bony and hard to hurt. Zombies, on the other hand, have more HP and a weakness to slashing damage‚Äîthey‚Äôre tougher, but their bodies aren‚Äôt built to deflect weapon attacks, and slashing attacks can rip them up quickly. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.485,t=0.356,r=0.862,b=0.571), with ID b5f2b6b3-e7e4-4523-bb7d-57bc37550396 -->

### SPEED
Your creature‚Äôs Speed should be 25 feet if it moves like a human. Beyond that, you can set the Speed to whatever makes sense. Remember that the creature can move up to triple this number if it spends its whole turn moving, so if you want the PCs to be able to chase the creature, its Speed can be only so high. Creatures at higher levels need ways to deal with flying PCs, speedy PCs, and PCs with more efficient actions that let them engage and retreat more easily. This might mean adding a fly Speed, giving the creature ranged attacks, and so forth. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.582,r=0.861,b=0.769), with ID ff54850b-b9d0-4b98-a404-eb817e808a24 -->

Creatures can have climb and swim Speeds even at
low levels. While you can give your creature a fly Speed
at those low levels, it‚Äôs better to wait until around 7th
level (when PCs gain access to fly) to give your creature
a fly Speed if it also has ranged attacks or another way
to harry the PCs from a distance indefinitely. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.771,r=0.861,b=0.863), with ID 57cb6980-c1d4-4295-ae77-c16354400b7d -->
