PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.014,r=0.375,b=0.042), with ID f4ddd94c-56d8-45a0-9af0-30db4cb8bbe4 -->

weapon; for unarmed attacks or weapons you invent, give
whatever traits you feel are appropriate. Note that these
traits might influence the damage you give the Strike.
  You might want to make sure a creature has an
unarmed attack if you think it‚Äôs likely to get disarmed.
At 7th level and higher, PCs might have the ability to
fly, which makes it more important for creatures to
have decent ranged Strikes to ensure they aren‚Äôt totally
hopeless against flying PCs (though they could instead
have fast fly Speeds or something similar). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.080,r=0.516,b=0.234), with ID 74c72093-b5b9-4581-9bfe-4c85eea2c82b -->

Strike Attack Bonus  
Use a high attack bonus for physically combative creatures‚Äîfighter types‚Äîthat also usually have high damage. A creature could have a higher attack bonus and lower damage, or vice versa (for instance, a moderate attack bonus and extreme damage might fit a creature that‚Äôs more like a barbarian), instead of having a poor statistic in another category. Spellcasters typically have poor attack bonuses, potentially in exchange for extreme spell DCs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.244,r=0.514,b=0.403), with ID 074388d5-da63-4d9b-ae7d-9a34601bc237 -->

STRIKE ATTACK BONUS
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Extreme</th><th>High</th><th>Moderate</th><th>Low</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>-1</td><td>+10</td><td>+8</td><td>+6</td><td>+4</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>+10</td><td>+8</td><td>+6</td><td>+4</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>+11</td><td>+9</td><td>+7</td><td>+5</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>+13</td><td>+11</td><td>+9</td><td>+7</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>+14</td><td>+12</td><td>+10</td><td>+8</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>+16</td><td>+14</td><td>+12</td><td>+9</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>+17</td><td>+15</td><td>+13</td><td>+11</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>+19</td><td>+17</td><td>+15</td><td>+12</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>+20</td><td>+18</td><td>+16</td><td>+13</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>+22</td><td>+20</td><td>+18</td><td>+15</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>+23</td><td>+21</td><td>+19</td><td>+16</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>+25</td><td>+23</td><td>+21</td><td>+17</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>+27</td><td>+24</td><td>+22</td><td>+19</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>+28</td><td>+26</td><td>+24</td><td>+20</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>+29</td><td>+27</td><td>+25</td><td>+21</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>+31</td><td>+29</td><td>+27</td><td>+23</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>+32</td><td>+30</td><td>+28</td><td>+24</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>+34</td><td>+32</td><td>+30</td><td>+25</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>+35</td><td>+33</td><td>+31</td><td>+27</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>+37</td><td>+35</td><td>+33</td><td>+28</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>+38</td><td>+36</td><td>+34</td><td>+29</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>+40</td><td>+38</td><td>+36</td><td>+31</td></tr><tr><td>21</td><td>+41</td><td>+39</td><td>+37</td><td>+32</td></tr><tr><td>22</td><td>+43</td><td>+41</td><td>+39</td><td>+33</td></tr><tr><td>23</td><td>+44</td><td>+42</td><td>+40</td><td>+35</td></tr><tr><td>24</td><td>+46</td><td>+44</td><td>+42</td><td>+36</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.414,r=0.512,b=0.849), with ID 12677197-e120-40fc-84e9-b0572a14f6a0 -->

**Strike Damage**  
The Strike Damage table gives the damage a creature should deal with a single Strike. You might use a lower category if the creature has better accuracy, or a higher category if its accuracy is lower. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.858,r=0.516,b=0.942), with ID 4c2593c1-f537-474d-94c7-d0e5dc0af017 -->

A creature that‚Äôs meant to be primarily a melee threat
uses high damage for its melee Strikes, or moderate for
melee Strikes that have the agile trait. Ranged attacks
more typically use the moderate value, or even low. A
creature that‚Äôs meant to be highly damaging uses the
extreme damage values, but might then have a moderate
attack bonus. As with most statistics, extreme damage is
more likely at higher levels. You can also use the extreme
value for special attacks that the creature can use only
a limited number of times or under circumstances that
aren‚Äôt likely to happen every round. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.080,r=0.906,b=0.249), with ID 07248225-5c67-4f99-8b34-4db08dccbe18 -->

More versatile creatures, such as ones that can
cast some spells and aren‚Äôt meant to primarily get
their damage through Strikes, go one category lower:
moderate for their main melee Strikes, low for agile
and ranged Strikes. Spellcasters and other creatures that
aren‚Äôt meant to be competent in a direct fight might use
the low damage value, or even less if they completely
don‚Äôt care about their Strikes. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.250,r=0.904,b=0.371), with ID c56a9aa3-51f9-46c7-a6f2-3eac3ba6dbe1 -->

The Strike Damage table entries include a damage expression (a die roll or rolls plus a flat modifier) you can use as is, or you can take the damage in parentheses and build your own damage expression to hit that number. If you do the latter, remember that a d4 counts as 2.5 damage, a d6 as 3.5, a d8 as 4.5, a d10 as 5.5, and a d12 as 6.5. Usually, a damage expression works best when roughly half the damage is from dice and half is from the flat modifier. If your creature deals special damage, like 1d6 fire from _flaming_ attacks, that counts toward its total damage per Strike. Keep in mind that a creature using a weapon should have a damage value that feels right for that weapon. Extreme damage works well for two-handed weapons that use d10s or d12s for damage. On the other hand, a dagger uses only d4s, so a dagger wielder would need something like sneak attack to deal extreme damage, or you might compensate for the dagger‚Äôs lower damage per Strike by giving the creature the ability to attack more efficiently or use other tricks. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.373,r=0.904,b=0.662), with ID 4698673a-b430-4363-912e-f906f6e4fcaa -->

Spells  
Your creature might have magical abilities that are best represented by spells. If you‚Äôre making a highly spellcasting-themed creature, give it prepared or spontaneous spells. For a creature that has spells due to its magical nature, especially if that magic isn‚Äôt its core focus, consider giving it some innate spells instead. How many spells you should give a creature depends on how you expect it to spend its actions in combat. If it‚Äôs primarily going to be making Strikes, it might not have any spells, or it might just have a few to help it move around better or protect against certain types of magic.

When choosing spells, lean hard into the creature‚Äôs theme. While many PCs choose spells to cover a wide <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.091,t=0.258,r=0.473,b=0.482), with ID 57bc5244-2da1-41fe-978d-4360757b4f96 -->

## MANUFACTURED WEAPONS
As noted in Items on page 116, most creatures have less
treasure than PCs, so those that rely on manufactured
weapons are significantly weaker if you don‚Äôt adjust
the weapons‚Äô damage to compensate. The method for
determining Strike damage on page 120 abstracts the
sources of damage, so you don‚Äôt have to worry about
adjusting the weapon‚Äôs damage. If you do decide to
calculate the weapon‚Äôs damage, give your creature
weapon specialization or greater weapon specialization
much earlier than a PC would get it. You might also
need to add sneak attack or similar abilities to make the
creature deal more damage. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.489,t=0.079,r=0.858,b=0.297), with ID c38e1a6d-0c06-4f41-a3e8-a94699a69421 -->

On the flip side, you might want to use a one-off
creature as a source of a particularly high-level piece of
treasure, such as a magic weapon. In these cases, you
might want to make the attack bonus higher for the
potency rune or the damage higher for a potent striking
rune so the PCs feel the weapon‚Äôs effect before they
obtain it. This will make the treasure feel more powerful
since they‚Äôve already been on the receiving end. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.495,t=0.296,r=0.855,b=0.423), with ID 319c7210-7879-409d-a3f4-92096d51b057 -->

variety of situations, creatures are more evocative the more
focused they are. Consider selecting about three-quarters <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.485,t=0.447,r=0.863,b=0.483), with ID 0c8e2cce-f0c4-418b-bd7f-a7b74011be6e -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.014,r=0.376,b=0.042), with ID e77417a3-0242-4d96-9c60-23e1d91d727b -->

of the spells based on relevance to the theme and the
remainder for other things. However, make sure the
spells aren‚Äôt all the same‚Äîselecting *fireball* for most of
a creature‚Äôs spell slots doesn‚Äôt make for a compelling fire
creature in the way a diverse selection of fire spells would. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.079,r=0.515,b=0.157), with ID 69021e13-4212-45d1-aa62-af89c463e3e1 -->

When choosing spells, some won‚Äôt be very useful if cast
at an extremely low rank compared to the creature‚Äôs level.
Most notably, damaging spells drop off in usefulness for
a creature that‚Äôs expected to last only a single fight. A
damaging spell 2 ranks below the highest rank a creature
of that level can cast is still potentially useful, but beyond
that, don‚Äôt bother. Spells that have the incapacitation trait
should be in the highest spell slot if you want the creature
to potentially get their full effect against PCs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.158,r=0.515,b=0.297), with ID 08f9414a-aeb4-4e4a-8d81-31d374907960 -->

## Spell DC and Spell Attack Modifier
Set the creature‚Äôs spell DC and spell attack modifier using the Spell DC and Spell Attack Modifier table on page 121. Most creatures use the same DC for all their spells, even if they have multiple types, such as a creature with both prepared spells and innate spells. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.305,r=0.513,b=0.402), with ID 8fe8ba9a-19a0-4605-9455-00c71c075496 -->

Use the high numbers for primary casters, and
the moderate numbers for creatures that have some
supplemental spells but are focused more on combat.
At 15th level and higher, the extreme numbers be¬≠
come standard for spellcasters. A few creatures might use the
extreme numbers at lower levels, but they tend to be
highly specialized, with very weak defenses and Strikes.
Secondary spellcasters can go up to high numbers if
they‚Äôre above 15th level and have offensive spells. There
is no low value‚Äîthe creature shouldn‚Äôt have any spells
in the first place if it would be that bad at using them! <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.403,r=0.513,b=0.572), with ID 237c9b07-7271-4011-a5bc-b597fd986814 -->

  ‚Ä¢ The anthropomorphic design and detailed attire indicate a fantasy setting, likely for a game or narrative context.
  ‚Ä¢ The character‚Äôs alert posture and expressive face enhance its personality and narrative potential. <!-- figure, from page 0 (l=0.081,t=0.575,r=0.525,b=0.959), with ID a5f3aaff-8c13-4279-907c-a548219551d2 -->

## Prepared and Spontaneous Spells
Spell slots work best for creatures that are meant to play like PC spellcasters. Choose the magical tradition best suited to the creature. You aren‚Äôt strictly limited to that tradition‚Äôs spell list, though sticking close to it will make your creature‚Äôs connection to that tradition more clear. The decision to use prepared or spontaneous spellcasting should align with the creature‚Äôs theme: a spontaneous spellcaster fits well as a one-off creature, since spontaneous spellcasting grants greater flexibility in the middle of battle, while a prepared spellcaster makes for a great recurring character who can change their spells between appearances. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.077,r=0.905,b=0.280), with ID 8f59b93b-368e-454f-a976-05ca9d14a44a -->

For a creature that can cast as many spells as a PC
spellcaster, the highest spell rank the creature can cast
is half its level rounded up. It gets five cantrips. If the
creature‚Äôs level is odd, it gets two spell slots of the
highest spell rank (plus three spell slots of each lower
rank), or three spell slots of that rank (plus four spell
slots of each lower level). If its level is even, it gets three
spell slots of the highest spell rank (plus three spell slots
of each lower rank), or four spell slots of that rank (plus
four spell slots of each lower rank). You can base the
number of spells on the class you are trying to emulate
or choose more spells if the creature doesn‚Äôt have many
other abilities. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.281,r=0.904,b=0.478), with ID a2d7feaa-4824-4996-9c8e-985c8ed9f0ab -->

Because creatures tend to be ‚Äúon stage‚Äù for only a short time, you usually don‚Äôt need to fill every spell slot. You can often fill just the top three ranks of spells, pick cantrips, and slot in a few thematic backup spells in the fourth rank down. For a recurring foe, you might give it a full complement of spells. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.480,r=0.903,b=0.571), with ID b30a179e-811d-4478-aa70-149d3a5ef46a -->

## Innate Spells
Unlike prepared and spontaneous spells, innate spells can
be of higher rank than half the creature‚Äôs level rounded
up, and you can choose how often they‚Äôre used‚Äîthey
can even be used at will or be constant effects. The most
notable innate spells tend to be top-rank ones that make
a big impact but can be used only once, at-will spells that
strongly reinforce the creature‚Äôs theme, and constant
spells that give it an ongoing benefit. A spell that‚Äôs
usable a limited number of times and has a lower
rank than the creature‚Äôs highest rank is typically less
likely to come up in combat; however, that‚Äôs a great
spot for utility and recovery spells, such as *dispel
magic* or *sound body*. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.583,r=0.903,b=0.800), with ID b37d244e-bf10-4d43-8fc9-6348d191fc5d -->

Sometimes a strongly thematic innate spell is of
a higher rank than the creature would normally be
able to cast, but it‚Äôs so fitting that it belongs there. Be
careful when doing this, as PCs might not have access
to the appropriate countermeasures for the spell.
This option works best for support, action denial, or
battlefield control spells that change the odds of a fight
without outright killing anyone, such as the succubus‚Äôs
*dominate* spell. These should make the fight more <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.802,r=0.903,b=0.939), with ID 066393d1-eecc-4f97-8dcc-48909c51698b -->

122 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.001,t=0.959,r=0.084,b=0.983), with ID f65fa9a8-0a1f-4b75-82d1-ff8404ba365d -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.686,t=0.014,r=0.872,b=0.048), with ID 2532f9aa-d09c-4205-821f-62c1314fdad0 -->

2 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.912,t=0.014,r=0.970,b=0.054), with ID 9e84f38f-aee8-4c99-96db-da4d994f04cf -->

interesting, not end it. Keep the number of such spells
very low, typically just one. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.090,t=0.078,r=0.473,b=0.111), with ID 53ae6454-5677-4174-a014-76d49a728b9e -->

Though you can achieve all sorts of things with innate
spells, always start with the theme and an idea of how
you want the creature to spend its actions. And though
you could give the creature a tool to counter every kind
of PC attack or trick, remember that the players chose
those options to enjoy using them, rather than to be
constantly foiled by an effectively invincible creature. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.092,t=0.112,r=0.474,b=0.221), with ID fd0051b1-bc26-4bf6-b34a-8984296792b2 -->

Rituals
Since rituals happen during downtime, giving them to a creature is usually a purely thematic choice. You can skip even looking at rituals in most cases. If you decide a creature needs to have a ritual for your story, add in the ritual whenever you need it. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.092,t=0.229,r=0.474,b=0.328), with ID 94dccf65-c7d6-4ef2-8aa3-16ec7da96e15 -->

## DESIGN ABILITIES
In this step, you‚Äôll take the ideas for abilities you noted when you developed your concept and design these abilities for your creature. You can look at existing creature abilities from *Monster Core* and feats from *Player Core* and use them as is or modify them to fit your needs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.335,r=0.474,b=0.433), with ID 566255fe-e499-403f-b50f-18fc22853eaa -->

When choosing abilities, think about both the number
of abilities and the diversity of abilities. Having a large
number of similar abilities can make the creature tougher
to run, and it probably can‚Äôt use them all anyway. A
diversity of abilities gives the creature different ways to
act in different situations, and helps guide you as the
GM. For instance, a combat creature might have one
ability it uses to get into position, another to use when
it wants to focus damage on a single enemy, and a third
that‚Äôs more defensive. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.092,t=0.433,r=0.474,b=0.588), with ID 94ea7fad-1c5f-46f3-9458-0143fc17534d -->

## Basics of Ability Design
There are a few principles of ability construction that you‚Äôll want to keep in mind. Some guidance for specific types of abilities will come later, but these apply to everything.

- ‚Ä¢ Respect the action economy.
- ‚Ä¢ Make sure abilities are level appropriate.
- ‚Ä¢ Avoid ‚Äúinvisible‚Äù abilities. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.596,r=0.474,b=0.727), with ID 90fae0c5-10a3-4c9b-b4f0-2b47f667ced4 -->

Action Economy  
Understanding a creature‚Äôs action economy is key to making it work in play. Remember how short the lifespan of a typical combat creature is. Including a bunch of combat abilities might mean you spend time building actions the creature will never have time to use. Narrow your selections down to the smallest and most compelling set that makes sense. Also keep in mind that special actions will compete for time with any combat spells you give the creature. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.736,r=0.474,b=0.894), with ID 7ddaaada-1836-48f0-aa34-8658401be415 -->

Reactions can help, giving the creature a way to act
when it‚Äôs not its turn. See Reactive Abilities on page 125
for advice on designing these tricky abilities. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.093,t=0.893,r=0.474,b=0.941), with ID bfc40678-1524-4f47-96db-b0c329520edb -->

## FOCUS SPELLS

Some creatures have focus spells, especially when those focus spells clearly fit a creature‚Äôs theme. Simply give the creature the focus spells you like and between 1 and 3 Focus Points (you can also allow your creature to cast focus spells using spell slots). Use the same DC and spell attack modifier as any other spell. A creature that has just 1 Focus Point is likely to cast a focus spell only once, unless it‚Äôs a recurring enemy. If the creature has plenty of spells already, you might want to skip focus spells altogether, as they aren‚Äôt as strong as top-rank spell slots. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.072,r=0.863,b=0.283), with ID dcd122cd-aad3-4eed-b14e-76ec7ecd6983 -->

Because of PC capabilities at higher levels, creatures
at those levels should get more abilities that improve
their action economy. For instance, creatures that
grapple should have Improved Grab instead of Grab,
Speeds should be higher, and many abilities that would
have cost an action at a lower level should be free
actions. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.292,r=0.865,b=0.404), with ID e3d391f4-0545-43d8-be12-7764ef9f86a7 -->

Level Appropriateness  
The effects of an ability should be appropriate to the creature‚Äôs level. For damaging abilities, that means they follow the damage guidelines on page 124. For others, take a look at spells and feats with a similar effect to see if they‚Äôre level appropriate. For instance, say you‚Äôre considering giving a 6th-level creature the ability to teleport a short distance. Translocate is comparable‚Äî that‚Äôs a 4th-rank spell, normally cast by a 7th-level or higher creature. That means 6th level probably isn‚Äôt too low, but the creature shouldn‚Äôt be able to use the ability more than once. You can also compare your creature to those in a Monster Core volume to see if the special abilities seem similar in power to those of other creatures of the same level. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.414,r=0.865,b=0.650), with ID d5eba824-c55e-4906-94b5-5fbd1691eb2e -->

Invisible Abilities  
Avoid abilities that do nothing but change the creature‚Äôs math, also known as ‚Äúinvisible abilities.‚Äù These alter a creature‚Äôs statistics in a way that‚Äôs invisible to the players, which makes the creature less engaging because the players don‚Äôt see it using its abilities in a tangible or evocative way. For example, an ability that allows a creature to use an action to increase its accuracy for the round with no outward sign (or worse, just grants a passive bonus to its accuracy) isn‚Äôt that compelling, whereas one that increases its damage by lighting its arrows on fire is noticeable. These both work toward the same goal‚Äîdealing more damage this round‚Äîbut one is far more memorable. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.485,t=0.658,r=0.865,b=0.880), with ID a5fecc32-780e-4511-8d39-b4d07b89f436 -->

**Active Abilities**  
Abilities a creature uses on its turn have the most flexibility and scope. You can use the Spell DC and Spell <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.484,t=0.887,r=0.864,b=0.943), with ID ff200e63-beb3-4c30-9b1a-36ff11b84572 -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.014,r=0.377,b=0.042), with ID 2f332496-a64d-4ca8-ad17-96f28a476920 -->

Attack Modifier table on page 121 to determine active
ability DCs as well as spell DCs. You can have an ability
use 1 to 3 actions as needed (or be a free action in rare
cases) and use just about any type of tactic. Feats, spells,
and existing creature abilities provide a wide variety of
examples, so look for something similar to your idea to
use as a basis. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.079,r=0.516,b=0.187), with ID 769a6454-a5c9-484a-9be8-1d4f578c87b2 -->

Consider how you want your creature to spend
its turns. Two-action activities pretty much define
the creature‚Äôs turn, and single actions work best for
supplemental benefits or normal Strikes. As you build
out your idea of a creature‚Äôs turn, don‚Äôt forget about
movement! A creature often needs to spend actions
getting into position, especially early in a fight. This
is especially challenging with melee-only creatures.
You can give such creatures abilities similar to Sudden
Charge or the deadly mantis‚Äôs Leaping Grab. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.189,r=0.514,b=0.341), with ID 9230d308-3206-4483-8577-655e00b0a952 -->

Use 3-action abilities sparingly, as a creature can‚Äôt
use them if it is slowed or stunned‚Äîmaking a creature‚Äôs
coolest or most defining ability use up 3 actions might
mean the creature never gets to use it. These activities
should be reserved for abilities that include some
movement (like Trample) or that the creature is likely
to use before engaging in combat. Don‚Äôt make an ability
use 3 actions as a way to balance it‚Äîsaying ‚ÄúThis can
be more powerful than other abilities because it is less
likely to work,‚Äù is a recipe for frustration if you‚Äôve made
a cool ability that‚Äôs too hard or even impossible for the
creature to use. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.342,r=0.514,b=0.524), with ID 253dd8a0-7645-45d2-bf7e-46d721e84ef1 -->

Be especially careful with activities when designing
boss creatures. They‚Äôre likely to get targeted with the
PCs‚Äô most powerful detrimental effects, get grabbed,
become slowed, or otherwise have their actions
restricted. Bosses need to have solid options they can use
with 1 or 2 actions. This lets them use their remaining
actions to get away, use a simple ability, or otherwise
keep the fight dynamic. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.526,r=0.513,b=0.648), with ID 97bf759f-1862-47fa-960c-6fb23bab9bdb -->

Free Actions  
Use free actions that don‚Äôt have triggers sparingly, and when you do, they should almost always be used for support or utility actions, not Strikes or movement. If you come up with a free action, consider whether it should be its own action or part of a combo, such as drawing a weapon and attacking. In cases like the latter, you might be better off making a single action that allows the creature to draw a weapon and then Strike. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.660,r=0.513,b=0.801), with ID dd34f4fd-b8bb-4e94-a132-364fd8f75ad2 -->

Damage-Dealing Abilities  
If a special action is a single action with only one target, you can often set damage using the Strike Damage table on page 120. If it uses more than 1 action or requires setup in some way, it might deal higher damage than is typical; often, you can just use the extreme column in these cases. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.814,r=0.513,b=0.922), with ID da4a6e75-7763-4e93-af5f-c20545dc27ec -->

For abilities that deal damage in an area, use the Area <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.154,t=0.924,r=0.512,b=0.939), with ID d768ec9b-333b-4566-9462-f9288e6df9c5 -->

Damage table. These numbers are based on a 2-action activity (e.g., most damaging spells). Single actions should deal much less damage. An ability that has another significant effect, like applying a condition, should deal less damage; for this, look at the damage for 2 or more levels lower, and judge which value would best match based on the severity of the additional effect. These abilities typically allow a basic saving throw. The table includes values for unlimited-use abilities (ones that can be used at will) and limited-use ones (which can be used once or, like dragon breath abilities, once or twice but not on consecutive turns). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.081,r=0.904,b=0.264), with ID d27afa39-2b91-494f-be8c-27969dc32968 -->

You can use the dice given or generate your own
expression based on the damage in parentheses, as
detailed in the Strike Damage section on page 120. If
a high-level effect has a small area compared to similar
abilities, you could have it deal more damage. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.266,r=0.904,b=0.341), with ID a3e1d3f5-8bae-4447-b80a-165ca0633f1f -->

## Defensive Abilities
Active offensive abilities usually fit creatures better
than defensive abilities do. Save defense increases for
creatures that are strongly defense-themed. For martial
creatures, something as simple as a shield and Shield
Block is usually plenty. Defensive abilities often run the
risk of being invisible abilities. For examples of good
defensive abilities, look at spells like _sanctuary_ for ideas,
or other spells that create interesting protective effects <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.797,r=0.904,b=0.939), with ID 853885b3-50ee-4d25-8cd6-d0b80e7446e1 -->

124 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.001,t=0.958,r=0.084,b=0.983), with ID b5f0ee62-91a7-4f9a-ad9d-63b7807861ee -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.689,t=0.018,r=0.870,b=0.043), with ID 889dd044-9f87-4189-9cd3-634b1fcd0dae -->

2 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.878,t=0.005,r=1.001,b=0.062), with ID 04aa2a5c-1ad1-41a1-a6b9-5cd6316b10a9 -->

instead of just granting a bonus. If you do want to make
a creature defensive, pick one defensive ability rather
than several, since stacking up multiple defenses can
make for a frustrating fight. One solid style of defensive
ability is a mode switch, which causes the creature to get
stronger defenses, but limits its attacks, spells, or other
offensive options. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.092,t=0.078,r=0.472,b=0.189), with ID f0c08c74-1432-4a12-b412-2d01c60f88a6 -->

Reactive Abilities  
Reactions and free actions with triggers can give a creature an impact outside of its turn. This can make the fight more interesting, but it may also be risky. It‚Äôs tempting to give every creature a reaction, but that‚Äôs not necessarily a good idea. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.198,r=0.472,b=0.295), with ID cbe0d375-c66e-412b-870d-dbec2418363e -->

To decide whether your creature should have a
reaction, first consider if the creature has the reflexes or
insight to react well in the first place‚Äîfor instance, an
ogre doesn‚Äôt have Reactive Strike because it‚Äôs a big oaf.
Oozes, constructs, and unintelligent creatures are less
likely to have reactions than others for this reason. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.296,r=0.471,b=0.387), with ID 6e0beea4-0b66-45d0-adbf-e9b05e4f3f23 -->

Second, look at the complexity of the encounter your
creature is likely to appear in. If you have a large number
of creatures, skipping reactions can make the fight flow
faster. A creature that‚Äôs more likely to fight solo, on the
other hand, might have a reaction to give it a way to
continue to be dangerous amid an onslaught of attacks
by the party. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.388,r=0.471,b=0.494), with ID ff071ad6-c751-4dc1-a3fb-e0e6c94da457 -->

When creating reactions, be careful with ‚Äúgotcha‚Äù
abilities‚Äîones that punish players for making perfectly
reasonable choices, for rolling poorly, and so on. If you
include abilities like this, they need to reinforce the
creature‚Äôs core theme and the play style you want it
to use in combat. For example, a creature that Strikes
as a reaction when someone fails an attack roll will
encourage PCs to use their actions on other tactics,
rather than attacking multiple times each turn. Is that
what you want? Is this dynamic essential for making the
creature feel like it‚Äôs supposed to? This isn‚Äôt the type of
ability you‚Äôd give to any old creature‚Äîonly an incredible
duelist or something similar. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.495,r=0.470,b=0.693), with ID cd7111f1-e3e7-4274-bdae-60bd937bc6df -->

Reactions should require something out of the ordinary to happen, or should be relatively weak if triggered by something ordinary. A reaction that triggers anytime someone tries to Strike a creature is likely to be perceived by the players as uninteresting because it‚Äôs so predictable. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.693,r=0.471,b=0.785), with ID 5eae0c0d-6e6c-4378-878f-eca197fd6f83 -->

The best reactions should be telegraphed so when they happen, it makes sense to the players. Think of one of the core reactions of the game: Shield Block. The creature raises its shield‚Äîan obvious action the PCs can see‚Äîso when it blocks damage from an attack, that makes perfect sense. Similarly, if you made a crystalline creature, you might have it build up sonic energy in a low thrum, so when it uses a reaction to release a burst of sonic energy when hit, the players can say, ‚ÄúOh, I should have seen that coming.‚Äù <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.786,r=0.471,b=0.938), with ID b4849429-b7c4-4183-bb93-155d3069f754 -->

Reaction Damage  
Reactions should use lower damage, usually that of a moderate Strike. A reaction that deals area damage might deal low damage, though use such reactions with caution. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.077,r=0.861,b=0.144), with ID 985cc964-2291-4b99-b323-292571e68409 -->

## Constant and Automatic Abilities
Certain abilities shouldn‚Äôt use any actions. Auras are a common constant ability, with frightful presence, the cinder rat‚Äôs fetid fumes, and the xulgath‚Äôs stench as notable examples. An aura needs a range, and if it needs a DC, you‚Äôll usually set it to the moderate spell DC unless the aura is one of the creature‚Äôs defining concepts. For example, the xulgath‚Äôs stench DC is significantly higher because the aura is such an iconic part of the creature. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.153,r=0.861,b=0.310), with ID ea85c752-9765-43df-a47a-de44aadd476c -->

Abilities the creature has no control over should be
automatic. For example, the living wildfire fire elemental
explodes into flames when it dies. It has no option not
to, so this wouldn‚Äôt make sense as a reaction or free
action. Conversely, the Ferocity ability is a reaction
because it requires the creature to give itself a last push
to stay at 1 HP. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.311,r=0.861,b=0.417), with ID 6d09bb6a-4880-4276-9f66-c70feab2c55b -->

## Constant and Automatic Damage
Much like for reactions, damage for a constant ability should be pretty low. Usually, this value is just below low Strike damage. Automatic abilities, like the living wildfire‚Äôs explosion ability, tend to deal moderate Strike damage or unlimited-use area damage. These abilities can deal even more if they happen only after the creature is dead or otherwise no longer presents a threat. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.430,r=0.862,b=0.557), with ID 58fe2ec0-ec4e-4d5c-9b0d-afbc3cb92e05 -->

Skill Abilities  
A skilled creature might have abilities related to its skills. The skill feats in *Player Core* make for a good baseline. Avoid giving your creature skill abilities that won‚Äôt matter in its interactions with PCs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.566,r=0.861,b=0.648), with ID 257e1086-f23f-4c9f-8df0-e4b4a3824e99 -->

Review
Now it‚Äôs time to look over your completed creature as a whole and make sure it‚Äôs living up to your concept. Can it do everything you wanted? Does it fit its intended role? Is there anything you could add or anything superfluous you could cut to get the creature where it needs to be? <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.659,r=0.860,b=0.769), with ID aa0a181d-4480-4bcf-b203-57abd169b55d -->

If this creature is built for combat, run through a
few turns in your head. Does it still work decently if it
gets slowed? Can it move into combat against the PCs
effectively considering their mobility options compared
to its own? Does it have any abilities it‚Äôll never use
because of its other actions? <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.771,r=0.860,b=0.861), with ID f8941ffd-7cec-4d83-b086-3828e20e43d9 -->

When you‚Äôre satisfied with your creation, it‚Äôs ready to
hit the table. But that‚Äôs not necessarily the end! If you
notice issues during the game, you can fix them on the
spot. It‚Äôs your game, and you can freely change what you
wrote if you think differently later on. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.863,r=0.860,b=0.938), with ID 40392a09-f15d-4fcf-b30a-9f2920de42bc -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.015,r=0.377,b=0.042), with ID 995335f8-aeee-4150-bc8e-666d5a65ac23 -->

## BUILDING NPCS
Creatures that are meant to cleave closely to character classes or intended to represent people rather than monsters are NPCs. They might face more scrutiny around their mechanics than creatures because a player can more directly compare their rogue to an NPC who acts like a rogue. That doesn‚Äôt mean you have to build an NPC exactly like a PC, though. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.136,t=0.075,r=0.516,b=0.203), with ID 3717ebf2-5e0a-41b0-b818-8ea698b9fea5 -->

You can build NPCs just like you would any other
creature. If an NPC should work like they have a class,
use the class features and feats of a suitable class to
pick abilities, and look at both the class‚Äôs proficiencies
and attribute modifiers to determine how strong the
NPC‚Äôs statistics should be. Class Road Maps on page
129 has prebuilt road maps for *Player Core* and *Player
Core 2* classes to get you started. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.204,r=0.515,b=0.326), with ID bde27e7c-522c-4e27-aa2c-3f0ddad0e17e -->

If the NPC isn‚Äôt meant to work like they have a class (a baker, for example), instead build the character separately. You can create new abilities as needed to get the NPC‚Äôs interactions with the PCs to express their theme and role in the story. These NPCs can be level ‚Äì1 or level 0. Their capabilities are below those of PCs, and they should typically not use any class features or feats from PC classes. Creatures of these levels tend to be extremely simple. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.326,r=0.515,b=0.463), with ID 76ce88cf-1e94-4bd4-9542-85a7a7068291 -->

It‚Äôs highly recommended that you select NPC skills
using proficiency ranks as you would a PC, though
you don‚Äôt need to be precise about the number of skill
increases you give the NPC. You can give them earlier
access to expert, master, or legendary proficiency if
they‚Äôre a skill-based NPC and better proficiency in
narrow areas of expertise, like Engineering Lore for a
tinker NPC. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.464,r=0.514,b=0.586), with ID 8b1a8631-e3af-4f1f-a59d-86794e06915b -->

Non-Combat Level  
An NPC‚Äôs level should represent their combat prowess. A common person might not be a combat threat, even if they‚Äôre important or highly skilled, and they consequently have a low level. However, that doesn‚Äôt mean they can‚Äôt present a challenge in other types of encounters. This is represented by a non-combat level (page 31) and tends to be specific to their area of expertise. For example, a barrister might be level ‚Äì1 in combat but a 4th-level creature in an encounter related to legal matters. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.598,r=0.513,b=0.769), with ID 22413925-6311-4ff8-a6f9-2540a423ff88 -->

This can go the other way as well, such as with a
powerful combat creature that‚Äôs not suited to social
settings. This is usually the case with creatures untrained
in mental skills. You can improvise this as you run the
game, or you can plan ahead if you have something
particular in mind. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.771,r=0.513,b=0.862), with ID a6ea68be-0aff-4cbd-93dc-ddbb11579f5b -->

Building an NPC‚Äôs non-combat level is relatively
simple. Choose the level you want the NPC to be for the
type of non-combat challenge you have in mind and use
the skill numbers for that level‚Äîtypically high or even
extreme. Some challenges, such as social challenges, <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.140,t=0.863,r=0.513,b=0.939), with ID f3ca936e-9fb9-45aa-8329-feb1ce19a518 -->

require the creature to have a high Perception and Will,
so in those cases, you should increase those values as
well. These should be set at the moderate or high values
for the non-combat level, usually, depending on how
adept you want the NPC to be. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.080,r=0.904,b=0.159), with ID c30d8e95-02b5-4fb9-9996-f21996d47f15 -->

Non-Combat XP  
The Experience Points gained for besting an NPC depend on how the party overcame them, because XP comes from overcoming a specific challenge. If the PCs defeat the NPC in a non-combat setting of the NPC‚Äôs specialty, the party gets XP based on the NPC‚Äôs non-combat level. If they just beat the NPC up, the XP would be based on the NPC‚Äôs creature level. Quite often, that means 0 XP and failure at the PCs‚Äô objective; for instance, during a baking contest, if the PCs murder the other baker, not only would they be disqualified, but they would likely be charged with a crime. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.169,r=0.905,b=0.357), with ID 547aec2e-6f40-410d-9bb1-a71341f04c89 -->

PC-style Build  
If you do choose to build an NPC fully using the PC rules, your NPC should generally end up being an appropriate challenge as a creature of their level. They will likely have lower statistics in some areas than if you had built them using the creature rules but more options due to their full complement of feats and class features. This is best saved for important, recurring NPCs, especially if they‚Äôre meant to engage in social or exploration endeavors rather than just battles. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.368,r=0.905,b=0.525), with ID a8fc81f2-c1a0-4598-9eb7-37c4ebafb9db -->

There are still some considerations and shortcuts that
can expedite the process while ensuring the NPC works
as intended. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.526,r=0.904,b=0.570), with ID ec05317f-c458-41a2-850f-7c56c0eb05ec -->

The creature‚Äôs treasure should follow the Treasure for New Characters rules on page 61. You‚Äôll need to account for this in your campaign‚Äôs overall treasure. You might even want to give the NPC a higher-level item appropriate as a treasure allotment for the level. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.542,t=0.571,r=0.904,b=0.662), with ID 30a63bd7-cca5-48b8-a9cf-ed8af7b1cd11 -->

You can expedite attribute modifier generation by making the starting attribute modifiers add up to +9, with no more than one modifier at +4 (and typically no more than one negative modifier). You can skip adding a background if you do this, but you might want to give the creature two skills, which includes one Lore skill, to represent the skills granted by a background. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.544,t=0.663,r=0.904,b=0.786), with ID 4ecb2bbb-ce56-40de-8ca5-3156702bc7c8 -->

- ‚Ä¢ It‚Äôs not necessary to assign every skill feat, particularly for a higher-level NPC. You can just pick the most emblematic ones and gloss over the rest. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.544,t=0.786,r=0.904,b=0.847), with ID ec2c78d9-9ed8-4d1b-9ce7-fb181919fe97 -->

- ‚Ä¢ For general feats, Incredible Initiative and Toughness
  make good choices. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.545,t=0.847,r=0.903,b=0.877), with ID d7854bfa-7d30-4964-9bf2-e3c4c77de6ca -->

- ‚Ä¢ Most of the guidelines about choosing spells still apply, though you might want a few more utility spells that deal with non-combat challenges, particularly in low-rank slots. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.545,t=0.877,r=0.904,b=0.938), with ID fd3194a7-e6de-4c91-9ddf-50fa082cb993 -->

128 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.002,t=0.959,r=0.083,b=0.983), with ID a93808ff-800c-490e-bb2a-ae6552e7c0be -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.688,t=0.018,r=0.871,b=0.044), with ID cf4194a1-96f3-49a3-a262-01049270f2e9 -->

2 <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.911,t=0.014,r=0.970,b=0.053), with ID 37051aa0-31dc-44c5-8fcb-12799376999d -->

CLASS ROAD MAPS  
You can use these suggestions when creating your road map to emulate a PC class, customizing as you see fit. You‚Äôll still need to look through the class to pick feats, weapons, and the like. Any statistic that isn‚Äôt specifically listed can use moderate numbers. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.107,t=0.089,r=0.460,b=0.188), with ID b2a3f4d3-97ef-4998-883a-582659417e1d -->

You don't need to give an NPC all the abilities from
its class‚Äîespecially those that just alter numbers. Each
class's entry lists several abilities that are good quick
choices and make for more interesting encounters.
This section includes the Player Core classes and those
from Player Core 2 (alchemist, barbarian, champion,
investigator, monk, oracle, sorcerer, and swashbuckler). <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.188,r=0.459,b=0.296), with ID df5fdbed-f478-4955-92f5-cc06fef52175 -->

Alchemist Low Perception; high Crafting; high Int, moderate or better Dex or Str; low to moderate HP; moderate attack with bombs. Abilities Infused alchemical items, Quick Bomber if a bomber alchemist, a few other alchemist abilities; it's usually easier to give the alchemist their bomb items rather than use Quick Alchemy on the spot. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.295,r=0.460,b=0.403), with ID 05374034-5a31-44aa-bb61-5257cc0dbf47 -->

Barbarian High Athletics; high Str, high to moderate Con; high AC; high Fortitude; high HP; moderate attack and extreme damage (when raging). Abilities Rage, instinct ability and related feats, raging resistance <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.403,r=0.460,b=0.463), with ID da835325-32f8-4b77-bde1-67d0fe901a69 -->

Bard Moderate Occultism, high Performance, high Charisma-based skills; high Cha; low Fortitude, moderate to high Will; low to moderate HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities spontaneous occult spellcasting as a bard of their level, composition spells, muse feats <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.463,r=0.460,b=0.555), with ID a5d2438d-fe3f-45a0-afab-1361f417b3a1 -->

Champion Low Perception; moderate Religion; high Str or Dex, moderate Cha; extreme AC; low Reflex; moderate attack and high damage. Abilities champion‚Äôs reaction, devotion spells, divine ally and related feats, divine smite, exalt, feats based on cause, Shield Block <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.556,r=0.460,b=0.632), with ID 2307391a-8fe7-4e28-803e-d4895ee6fecb -->

Cleric (Cloistered Cleric) High Perception; high Religion, moderate or high skill themed to deity; low AC; high Wis; low Fortitude, high Will; low to moderate HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities Prepared divine spellcasting as a cleric of their level; divine font; domain spells <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.632,r=0.459,b=0.724), with ID 3ad7736c-ee08-4521-b956-6e9ffb060810 -->

Cleric (Warpriest) Moderate Perception; moderate Religion, moderate or high skill themed to deity; high Str, moderate Wis; high AC; low Reflex, high Will; high spell DC. Abilities prepared divine spellcasting as a cleric of their level; divine font, Shield Block <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.108,t=0.724,r=0.459,b=0.801), with ID 573f6b5a-b56d-49a0-a2e0-ee24466f66c2 -->

Druid High Perception; high Nature, moderate or high skill from order; high Wis; high Will; low to moderate HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities Prepared primal spellcasting as a druid of their level; order ability, order feats, and order spell for their order; Shield Block; add an animal to the encounter for animal order <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.109,t=0.801,r=0.460,b=0.907), with ID fa291b9b-78cf-4b5e-87c8-a36c9f5a3cdd -->

Fighter High Acrobatics or Athletics; high Str or Dex; high AC; low Will; high attack and high damage. Abilities Bravery, Reactive Strike, Shield Block, a few fighter abilities and feats associated with a combat style <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.110,t=0.907,r=0.459,b=0.972), with ID 34ddc889-5078-4944-a17f-b75d02e7b83d -->

Investigator  High Perception; high Society, high methodology skill, plus more skills than usual; high Int; low Fortitude, high Will; low to moderate HP; moderate attack and low to moderate damage before strategic strike plus high damage with strategic strike. Abilities Pursue a Lead, Devise a Stratagem, methodology action <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.502,t=0.094,r=0.848,b=0.188), with ID e091af30-79d8-4d2c-8f8c-6ce10f3839ee -->

Monk High Acrobatics, Athletics, or both; high Str or Dex, moderate Wis; high or extreme AC; moderate attack and high damage. Abilities Flurry of Blows, metal strikes, mystic strikes, perfected form, stance and related feats, ki abilities <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.502,t=0.188,r=0.849,b=0.265), with ID 504cd308-49dc-4e99-85df-b8bd905d484b -->

Oracle High Religion, high mystery skill, moderate or high skill themed to deity; high Cha; low AC; low Fortitude, high Will; low to moderate HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities Spontaneous divine spellcasting as an oracle of their level; oracle mystery and related benefit, cantrip, and revelation spells, constant minor curse, activate moderate curse after casting a revelation spell <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.265,r=0.849,b=0.387), with ID ff5f3e99-7161-4fa3-a0e1-3f52355a6b21 -->

Ranger High Perception; moderate Nature and moderate to high Survival; high Str or Dex; high AC; moderate attack and high damage (or for a simpler ranger, remove Hunt Prey, and just use high attack and high damage). Abilities Hunt Prey, hunter‚Äôs edge, nature‚Äôs edge, masterful hunter, swift prey, companion or combat style and related feats <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.387,r=0.850,b=0.494), with ID f9e0f7c3-dc0d-428e-af98-236468675ac5 -->

Rogue High Perception; high Stealth and Thievery, plus more skills than usual; high Dex (or key attribute for a specific rogue‚Äôs racket); high AC; low Fortitude, high Reflex; low to moderate HP; moderate attack and low to moderate damage before sneak attack plus high or extreme damage with sneak attack. Abilities Sneak attack, surprise attack, deny advantage, debilitating strike, master strike, rogue‚Äôs racket and related feats <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.494,r=0.850,b=0.617), with ID f5a97494-2c38-4ef3-88fc-4c62a54dbad3 -->

Sorcerer Low Perception; moderate bloodline skills and high Charisma-based bloodline skills; high Cha; low AC; low Fortitude; low HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities Spontaneous spellcasting of a tradition based on bloodline as a sorcerer of their level, bloodline and related feats and spells <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.502,t=0.618,r=0.849,b=0.709), with ID f0b5ed9e-7285-4044-9ea6-4a1ee950ffab -->

Swashbuckler High Perception; high Acrobatics, plus high style skill; high Dex; high AC; low Fortitude, high Reflex; moderate HP; moderate attack and low to moderate damage before precise strike plus high or extreme damage with precise strike. Abilities Panache, precise strike, Confident Finisher, swashbuckler‚Äôs style (plus exemplary finisher at 9th level) <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.709,r=0.850,b=0.816), with ID 8b3c3b77-e027-4077-8374-bbe2ba265ef2 -->

Witch Low Perception; high patron skill; high Int; low AC; low Fortitude; low HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities prepared spellcasting of a tradition based on patron as a witch of their level, familiar, witch‚Äôs patron and related cantrip, hex, and spells <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.503,t=0.816,r=0.850,b=0.891), with ID 605ec942-f607-4cea-aefa-f6afb8c6b5ec -->

Wizard Low Perception; high Arcana; high Int; low AC; low Fortitude; low HP; low accuracy; high to extreme spell DC. Abilities prepared arcane spellcasting as a wizard of their level, Drain Bonded Item, school spell, and curriculum spells <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.502,t=0.892,r=0.848,b=0.972), with ID 469f2e97-d5e5-4d82-908e-6f0fc25169cb -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.113,t=0.014,r=0.376,b=0.042), with ID 66795e70-e256-4ce9-82c7-3dfab08a1631 -->

# BUILDING ITEMS

*Creating your own magic and alchemical items is an amazing way to customize the adventure and gameplay for your group and add unique elements without requiring quite the same mechanical depth as a whole new class, archetype, or ancestry.* <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.079,r=0.908,b=0.167), with ID 9515216a-b529-4f2e-a282-d313287a4692 -->

New items make great mementos of previous adventures  
and tend to be one of the easiest elements for a character to  
begin using mid-campaign after receiving them as a reward.  
This section explains the philosophy and numbers behind  
creating items so you can design your own in no time! <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.203,r=0.514,b=0.281), with ID 0563cbbe-cdab-4f13-afb7-815b25fee1e1 -->

## CONCEPT AND ROLE
First, come up with a concept for the item based on the role the item serves in your game and in the game‚Äôs world. You might include a new item in an ancient ruin to hint at its history and characterize the people who used to live there. For instance, a Thassilonian ruin might have an item based on rune magic, while a Jistkan ruin might have an item related to the empire‚Äôs ancient constructs. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.290,r=0.514,b=0.418), with ID 4911c9f9-3faa-487e-8d9f-aa5478c71963 -->

A new magic item might be important later in the story,
or its role might be as simple as a fun wolf-themed item for
the monk that uses Wolf Stance. Keep your concept in mind
to guide you through the process. Start thinking about what
kind of magic item it will be. Each item type has its own
niche, and some are less likely to be as useful to the PCs. For
instance, new weapons and armor require the PC to give up
the weapon or armor they already have, which might make
them more reluctant to use the new items unless they‚Äôre
noticeably better, while consumable items don‚Äôt have as big
an impact on the story as permanent items. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.419,r=0.514,b=0.587), with ID 92b974e9-1bd7-4733-9621-37498b0b6948 -->

Item Level  
A new item is typically going to be within a few levels of the PCs. If it‚Äôs too low, it might not be interesting, and if it‚Äôs too high, it might be too powerful or too lucrative to sell. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.598,r=0.514,b=0.665), with ID 94f49f29-86aa-41fe-ad67-57f5be236bcb -->

Comparison
First, look at similar items. For example, if you want a permanent item that lets someone fly, look at the *flying broomstick*, which moves of its own volition to a location and thus can‚Äôt be used to gain a huge advantage in combat, and *winged sandals*, which can. This will give you an idea of the right level range and the specifics and limitations of existing items. You might even be able to just adjust one of those to get what you want with minimal work. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.675,r=0.513,b=0.817), with ID d65d6c14-1a75-4ba8-8ae7-109485e6e428 -->

Item Effects  
Next, use the item‚Äôs concept and role to decide its effects.  
This is where your creativity will bring the item to life.  
Make sure to have it do something exciting and roleplay-  
inspiring. A magic item that does nothing more than  
deliver a bonus is far less interesting, even if the item does  
have a load-bearing item bonus, like a magic weapon. To <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.828,r=0.513,b=0.940), with ID 88ce8114-6b01-4fb0-aebb-defc00f4b930 -->

determine the item‚Äôs power, take into account the special
abilities you give the item as well as the item bonus (if any)
that it grants. For specific advice for the type of magic item
you are creating, check out Designing by Type on page 131. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.203,r=0.904,b=0.266), with ID c0c8fa36-9415-439b-b4fe-f5703c867db6 -->

Special Abilities  
When deciding what special abilities are appropriate for what level, it‚Äôs best to look for similar spells to gauge the effect. For most consumables, the effect should be less powerful than the highest rank spell a spellcaster of the item‚Äôs level could cast. Scrolls are about the most efficient you can get‚Äîthey‚Äôre the same level the spellcaster would be‚Äîbut they require a spellcaster that has the spell on their list, and take the same actions as casting the spell normally. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.277,r=0.903,b=0.417), with ID 827790f4-dc7a-427b-b94c-23cb6fe020a3 -->

The most straightforward choice is a once-per-day
ability. For this, the item‚Äôs level should be at least 2 levels
higher than the minimum level a spellcaster could first cast
that spell. For example, if your ability is about as powerful
as a 3rd-rank spell cast once per day (perhaps haste),
then it should be at least a 7th-level item. A basic wand
is a good example. However, a wand is flexible and can
contain the most effective possible choice for its spell rank
(such as long-lasting spells where once a day is effectively
permanent), so a specific item that doesn‚Äôt grant such a
spell could have additional powers or bonuses at the same
price as a wand. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.418,r=0.903,b=0.601), with ID 793e9ef8-3a54-46ce-8b55-920951ed253b -->

If the item can be activated multiple times per day, it
should be at least 4 levels higher instead‚Äî9th level in our
example. Frequency could range from twice per day to
once per hour and anything in between. Choose whatever
makes sense to allow the characters to use the item more
frequently without being effectively constant or unlimited.
The appropriate frequency, or whether it‚Äôs ever okay to
have unlimited activations, varies wildly based on the spell.
Unlimited castings of a cantrip is fine, but an effect akin to
a non-cantrip spell is rarely a good idea. Only attempt to
build such an item when you‚Äôre certain of the consequences. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.604,r=0.902,b=0.769), with ID 307f7aa8-1d98-4644-8c0d-cc934a0d6eca -->

Items that can be activated less often than once per
day don‚Äôt appear too often, and they usually fit best with
abilities that make sense outside of encounters. It‚Äôs still
best to stick to the guidelines for once-per-day abilities,
but these items tend to have more properties‚Äîand often
strange ones. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.532,t=0.771,r=0.903,b=0.862), with ID 82a62c13-bdcd-4146-97d6-bb6573a5bdfd -->

Constant Abilities  
If you want an effect to be constant, set the level and Price accordingly. For instance, let‚Äôs say your group is 16th level and you want to give them an item themed around flying. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.532,t=0.876,r=0.902,b=0.939), with ID 3e9f6ff9-1521-4cef-8181-6c413eefa1c1 -->

130 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.003,t=0.960,r=0.083,b=0.982), with ID ba9ce548-af14-4a88-95d7-37b3b948d32c -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.689,t=0.019,r=0.869,b=0.043), with ID 295ecdb6-e0d8-4788-90c6-dab9e3d175f1 -->

A 7th-rank *fly* spell lasts an hour already, so one casting
covers a significant portion of the adventuring day. To keep
it simpler, you decide to create a 16th-level cloak that lets
the wearer constantly fly. Remember, some effects were
never meant to be constant and could warp your game. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.078,r=0.471,b=0.160), with ID 3895733b-d547-4b72-908f-2d8368a73574 -->

Activation Actions
Watch out when picking the number of actions an activation takes! A 1-action activation that casts a spell with a 2-action casting time is drastically more powerful in an encounter than an item with a 2-action activation would be. An item like that is typically much higher level, and it works best with ‚Äúhelper‚Äù spells or ones with limited utility rather than offensive spells. The safest bet is to use the same number of actions the spell normally takes to cast. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.169,r=0.471,b=0.312), with ID 105f803a-2749-403b-aa20-82159e014187 -->

Scaling out of Usefulness  
Some spells aren‚Äôt appealing if their rank is too low. For instance, an item that casts 1st-rank *breathe fire* three times per day might be 5th or 6th level. The problem is that spell scaling has the biggest impact at low levels, so the spell isn‚Äôt effective compared to other actions a character could take. Err on the side of fewer, more impressive activations. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.323,r=0.471,b=0.435), with ID 361b1901-f07d-4121-82f2-1aaf3fa26dd4 -->

Bonuses
If your item includes item bonuses, check the table below for the minimum item levels the game‚Äôs math expects permanent bonuses to be applied to. A lower-level item might give such a bonus temporarily, but keep track to make sure the item isn‚Äôt effectively permanent. If a character typically picks three or fewer locks a day, there‚Äôs no difference between a +2 item bonus to pick all locks and an activation that gives a +2 item bonus to Pick a Lock three times per day. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.445,r=0.470,b=0.601), with ID 1b36330b-d986-4e0e-9af5-0a58cec099f5 -->

For attack bonuses, AC, and saves, the minimums match _runic weapon_ and _magic armor_. You can have other items with these bonuses (like _handwraps of mighty blows_), but keep in mind they compete with fundamental runes. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.603,r=0.470,b=0.663), with ID d144eb72-29cf-4758-bcd6-49edd234ca64 -->

Skill bonuses come on a wider range of items. Some are
more broadly useful, so an Athletics item might be more
expensive than an equivalent Society item. Gaining a bonus
to Perception is especially valuable compared to gaining a
bonus to a skill. Just because an item is the minimum level
for its bonus doesn‚Äôt mean the bonus should be the item‚Äôs
only power. The item can and should have an additional
interesting power beyond the bonus. Likewise, an item can
come at a higher level than the minimum, but if it‚Äôs much
higher, its abilities start to compete with the next bonus. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.664,r=0.470,b=0.817), with ID aecacf81-95ca-42d2-8475-355809143b84 -->

LEVELS FOR PERMANENT ITEM BONUSES
<table><thead><tr><th>Statistic</th><th>+1</th><th>+2</th><th>+3</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Attack bonus</td><td>2</td><td>10</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><td>AC</td><td>5</td><td>11</td><td>18</td></tr><tr><td>Save (resilient rune)</td><td>8</td><td>14</td><td>20</td></tr><tr><td>Skill/Perception</td><td>3</td><td>9</td><td>17*</td></tr><tr><td colspan="4">* This is also the minimum level for apex items.</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.097,t=0.828,r=0.469,b=0.940), with ID bfcdf3dd-800a-4857-94c1-7413dd781228 -->

**DESIGNING BY TYPE**  
The following guidance applies to items of various types. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.077,r=0.859,b=0.113), with ID 65f5eb28-7a5f-43f6-9ada-a2201c0f01a0 -->

Alchemical Items  
Alchemical items are consumables. Because alchemists can make a large number for free, alchemical items tend to be on the weaker end for their level, with lower Prices. Avoid alchemical effects that feel too much like magic. Alchemy is capable of fantastical things, but should have its own distinct feel; where you draw the line depends on your game. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.123,r=0.860,b=0.249), with ID 209c8e2c-1cf1-4467-a386-5ec424160151 -->

Alchemical bombs are like weapons for alchemists
and should primarily deal damage, with small extra
effects. Existing bombs are great models. Elixirs are
varied; make sure not to duplicate potions, especially
highly magical ones. Poisons are one of the trickier
alchemical items to make, and it‚Äôs usually best to just
tweak one found on pages 248‚Äì250 to avoid making
something that‚Äôs overpowered; compare to poisons of
the same type that have similar onset and stage duration,
as longer onset and duration poisons tend to deal
drastically more damage. Alchemical tools are best used
for adding a little weirdness. They can be creative and
interesting, but tend not to be powerful. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.250,r=0.861,b=0.448), with ID 30c401af-38e2-439a-b8f4-bea214e8b107 -->

Ammunition
Magic ammunition is consumable; launching it destroys it. Pay attention to whether you give the ammunition an activation: any big flashy effect for its level should almost always have one, since otherwise the effect is essentially a free action on top of a Strike. This is particularly important for extremely low-level ammunition, since a high-level character could use that ammunition for every Strike without noticing the gold cost. If the ammunition doesn‚Äôt deal normal Strike damage on a hit, remember to say that! Dealing damage is the default. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.461,r=0.861,b=0.633), with ID 8faf8447-1279-4128-a79b-500f68666b39 -->

Armor and Weapons  
Specific armor and weapons replace the opportunity to add property runes, so you have a lot of space to design. Choose abilities that feel attached to the fact that they are weapons or armor; for instance, a fiery sword that you point at an enemy to shoot fire bolts is more on theme than a fiery sword that casts wall of fire in an unconnected way. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.645,r=0.859,b=0.755), with ID fd8c9d00-ea63-4fba-b511-b5d975ac0d63 -->

The specific item should cost more than the base armor or weapon would with just the fundamental runes, but you can often discount the cost of the additional components significantly as part of the specific item‚Äôs special niche. Be careful about specific armor or weapons that include property runes in addition to unique specific abilities. If you discount the item, you might end up with an item significantly superior to one built using the normal property runes system. That‚Äôs not always bad, since it‚Äôs still giving up customization for power, and this can be appropriate if the item has an important place in your story. Just make sure the difference isn‚Äôt too drastic. If you just want to <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.757,r=0.859,b=0.938), with ID bde16098-fdc1-4f53-9346-93f0dac56c7c -->

PATHFINDER GM CORE <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.111,t=0.014,r=0.376,b=0.044), with ID 1691de5d-4b57-4328-90bc-57292619f5d4 -->

POTIONS VS. ELIXIRS

Alchemical items and magic items follow a similar price economy based on their level, but the effects of potions can be a bit broader and more directly magical. Alchemists can also make extremely large numbers of elixirs at an item level equal to their alchemist level, so if a 17th-level elixir was competing with a 9th-rank spell for power, an alchemist would be packing the equivalent of over 40 9th-rank spells, and potentially quickened spells thanks to the single action activation. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.073,r=0.514,b=0.266), with ID f14cc9b0-940f-44e9-bf41-92fcbd701bc2 -->

create armor or a weapon with runes and no extra special
abilities, you can do so. The Price of such an item is the sum
of all the runes‚Äô Prices, and its level is that of the highest-
level rune on the item. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.137,t=0.280,r=0.514,b=0.341), with ID 07485214-758f-4ef8-935d-dd31f1c858a5 -->

When picking abilities, you can also consider taking from
the relic gifts found on pages 309‚Äì319. Even if your game
doesn‚Äôt use relics, that section has plenty of choices sorted
by theme. If you do, keep in mind that relic abilities are
typically more powerful than usual for their level and that
those abilities wouldn‚Äôt scale on a normal magic item. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.138,t=0.341,r=0.514,b=0.435), with ID 03f7c26b-f7d8-4b4b-b241-5870c0f6756e -->

Held Items  
Usually, held items should require manipulation to use, with Interact activations. They are most often tools, implements, items that can be thrown, and the like. Imagine a PC physically using the item and what that looks like. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.445,r=0.514,b=0.540), with ID fd12e2c4-1dde-4ba5-a080-fb1e5ef5bb35 -->

Remember that held items are more challenging for martial characters to use, compared to spellcasters or hands-free characters, like monks. A barbarian might have to give up a two-handed weapon to use a held item, and so is less likely to use one. This means you might want to design held items specifically for non-martial characters, or have them be items a martial character uses outside of combat. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.541,r=0.514,b=0.648), with ID 2b7a7a74-94cc-40d4-931d-6d9296e0e57b -->

Oils
Oils are consumables you slather onto items or, rarely, creatures. They provide an interesting opportunity to apply effects to other items. Just remember not to accidentally make something that should be applied topically into a potion; for instance, a petrified character can‚Äôt drink an anti-petrifying potion! The actions an oil takes to use depend on how thoroughly it needs to be applied. For one used outside of combat, it could take a minute or more. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.659,r=0.513,b=0.816), with ID 7c43d6c8-4bd5-4180-9bde-7fe6d866890c -->

Potions
Potions are consumables in the truest sense; you literally consume them. Since the action of drinking isn‚Äôt easy to split up, they take only a single action to activate. This advantage makes potions that replicate spell effects incredibly powerful, and it‚Äôs the reason potions are nearly always higher level than scrolls with similar effects. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.139,t=0.828,r=0.513,b=0.940), with ID 5f38ee28-6116-4107-92a8-ecca10f7ce2e -->

Runes
Property runes are a fun and versatile way to customize
weapons and armor without throwing away the previous
items. Each should be fairly simple, especially at lower levels,
because combining runes can make things overcomplicated.
Compare to other properties to determine the right level. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.077,r=0.905,b=0.176), with ID 9b27572f-65b3-4553-b53d-e06ee6d1ae37 -->

Scrolls
You‚Äôll never need to design a new scroll, but use them as a comparison when designing other types of consumables. If you‚Äôre designing a consumable that seems like it‚Äôs much better than a scroll of its level‚Äîor faster to activate‚Äîyou should probably raise the item‚Äôs level or adjust the effect. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.184,r=0.905,b=0.281), with ID 146aedfd-027c-4d9d-8420-68fe275a6e67 -->

Shields
Use the sturdy shields as benchmarks for the best possible shield Hardness, HP, and BT for a shield of that level. Your new shield should have less than those benchmarks since it also does something else, and you can use the magnitude of the reduction to build room for creative defensive abilities. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.529,t=0.291,r=0.905,b=0.389), with ID 6db3ce45-562d-4296-a6a2-34c3bbe2b521 -->

Staves  
You‚Äôll need to come up with a theme and curate a list of spells that stay close to that theme, typically one to three per spell rank, all on one spell list. A staff is always at least 3 levels higher than the minimum level for a spellcaster to cast the highest-rank spell it contains, so a staff with up to 4th-rank spells would be at least a 10th-level item. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.400,r=0.905,b=0.511), with ID af062cb8-2497-4911-9e4f-85b82c7fad0f -->

Structures
Structures are evocative and make great tertiary items, quirky but not part of a combat build. This allows you to price them affordably, but make sure there isn‚Äôt some hidden abuse where the structure drastically alters encounters. The structure trait is intended to help as a starting point. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.522,r=0.904,b=0.618), with ID 15836905-833c-43dc-a7da-950cc0a41734 -->

Talisman

Because talismans are affixed ahead of time but don‚Äôt take an action to retrieve, they reward forethought and planning. Those that can be activated as a free action also have the best action efficiency of any consumable. In the same way scrolls reward specific spellcasters, talisman requirements reward particular types of characters. Talismans might grant a single use of a feat, with an additional effect if the character already has that feat. Think of talismans as martial characters‚Äô answer to scrolls to expand on the options of the non-spellcasters at your table. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.531,t=0.629,r=0.905,b=0.801), with ID 190b4197-a35b-47a3-b664-91fd013e724a -->

**Wands**  
You won‚Äôt need to design basic *magic wands*, but you might want a special wand. When designing a new special wand, your wand‚Äôs level will usually be 1 to 2 levels higher than the basic wand, depending on the magnitude of the special effect. Remember that if you make the wand 2 levels higher, it‚Äôs now competing with wands of a spell a whole level higher, so the special effect should be worth that cost! <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.530,t=0.813,r=0.904,b=0.940), with ID 5a3265fa-042c-4f4c-acd1-414768adaa63 -->

132 <!-- marginalia, from page 0 (l=0.001,t=0.959,r=0.084,b=0.983), with ID 53962b9d-569a-4461-aca8-b1aa557afe76 -->

BUILDING GAMES <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.690,t=0.019,r=0.868,b=0.043), with ID 59eca91d-0e15-4eb7-b5ea-b0623b40c7df -->

Worn Items
Worn items vary wildly in their effects, but they all take up one of a character‚Äôs 10 invested items. Remember to include the item‚Äôs worn entry, if applicable (or ‚Äú‚Äî‚Äù if you could imagine someone wearing 10 or more with no difficulty). Where the item is worn should usually match its effects or bonuses: shoes help you move, eyepieces affect your vision, and so on. As with held items, imagine a character wearing the item to picture how they use its magic. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.094,t=0.075,r=0.471,b=0.218), with ID 4fa5e16c-9bb4-4e1f-85a2-68db2540e863 -->

Apex items are always at least level 17 and should have
unique abilities on top of their bonus, just like other items. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.219,r=0.470,b=0.251), with ID 24354fc1-bccb-42b9-933c-9fc1b7ba1f74 -->

## Fill in the Numbers
You‚Äôre almost done! The final step is to fill in the numbers. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.260,r=0.469,b=0.296), with ID 95cf4141-1fc5-45f3-b2e9-c5c73841c819 -->

DCs
Choose any DCs for the item‚Äôs abilities, typically using the typical DCs in the table below. An item with a narrow function might have a DC up to 2 higher, and one that forces a save (such as with an aura) is typically 2 lower. The lower the DC, the quicker the item becomes obsolete. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.095,t=0.306,r=0.470,b=0.403), with ID 646c0cff-b845-4db0-b4f5-9703084973bd -->

MAGIC ITEM DCS
<table><thead><tr><th>Item Level</th><th>DC</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>17</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>18</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>19</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>20</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>23</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>24</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>25</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>27</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>28</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>29</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>30</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>31</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>34</td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>35</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>37</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>38</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>41</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>43</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.097,t=0.414,r=0.469,b=0.759), with ID f2350f00-ba17-4119-a94c-58934dc190ee -->

Item Prices  
Each item level has a price range. Based on the item‚Äôs role and abilities, decide where in that range to place it. There‚Äôs plenty of variation, and you primarily need to worry about Price only if you expect the PCs will be able to sell it. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.096,t=0.767,r=0.470,b=0.847), with ID 8e61064c-ee38-48dc-9a6d-45fc454bf460 -->

**Primary items** cost near the highest value for their level. They have a big impact on combat or player ability. This includes weapons, armor, and Perception items. The highest price is for items like *runic weapon*, *magic armor*, and apex items. So a *+1 striking weapon* is 100 gp at 4th level.

**Secondary items**, with middle values, give significant <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.097,t=0.847,r=0.470,b=0.938), with ID f1f1a856-6c03-43a7-96a6-06d0e0bf565a -->

secondary benefits or enhance highly consequential
noncombat or support skills like Medicine or Crafting.

Tertiary items, with low value, are weird or very specific
items, ones not usually core to a character‚Äôs build. Especially
strange ones might fall into the gap between two levels. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.487,t=0.081,r=0.860,b=0.158), with ID dc4bf700-e8c3-42f1-a449-f050fbf389a3 -->

PERMANENT MAGIC ITEM PRICE
<table><thead><tr><th>Level</th><th>Price</th><th>Core Item</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1</td><td>10-20 gp</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>25-35 gp</td><td>+1 weapon</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>45-60 gp</td><td>+1 skill item</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>75-100 gp</td><td>+1 striking weapon</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>125-160 gp</td><td>+1 armor</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>200-250 gp</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>300-360 gp</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>415-500 gp</td><td>+1 resilient armor</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>575-700 gp</td><td>+2 skill item</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>820-1,000 gp</td><td>+2 striking weapon</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>1,160-1,400 gp</td><td>+2 resilient armor</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>1,640-2,000 gp</td><td>+2 greater striking weapon</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>2,400-3,000 gp</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>3,600-4,500 gp</td><td>+2 greater resilient armor</td></tr><tr><td>15</td><td>5,300-6,500 gp</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>16</td><td>7,900-10,000 gp</td><td>+3 greater striking weapon</td></tr><tr><td>17</td><td>12,000-15,000 gp</td><td>+3 skill item, apex item</td></tr><tr><td>18</td><td>18,600-24,000 gp</td><td>+3 greater resilient armor</td></tr><tr><td>19</td><td>30,400-40,000 gp</td><td>+3 superior striking weapon</td></tr><tr><td>20</td><td>52,000-70,000 gp</td><td>+3 superior resilient armor</td></tr></tbody></table> <!-- table, from page 0 (l=0.489,t=0.169,r=0.859,b=0.511), with ID 222e3c91-4312-4855-beb9-be90941b4a2a -->

Consumables

Consumables have a slightly narrower range, with top-end items like scrolls, optimum healing potions, or super-useful consumables like a *potion of invisibility* at the high end. <!-- text, from page 0 (l=0.488,t=0.523,r=0.859,b=0.587), with ID f2a80873-a621-4a6e-9c46-71f4f69aa932 -->

CONSUMABLE PRICE
